Preface
This thesis will count for 32.5 ECTS points and was completed to obtain a Master Degree
in Biotechnology at Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The studies was conducted
at Statens Serum Institut (SSI) under the supervision of Senior Scientist Thomas Lin-
denstrøm (SSI) with support from Professor Gregers Jungers (DTU). All experimental
work was carried out at the Department of Infectious Disease Immunology (SSI) during
a six month period from August 2017 to February 2018.
This thesis primary purpose is to be the base for my supervisors evaluation of my edu-
cational work. Secondary provide information for others with interest in the ﬁeld of Tu-
berculosis and vaccine research in relation to gene expression analysis of vaccine-primed
CD4+ T cells.
Copenhagen, February 6, 2018
Marie Louise Nautrup Therkelsen

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Tuberculosis (TB) is an infectious lung disease and a leading cause of death worldwide,
killing over a million people every year [1]. TB is caused by infection with the intercellular
bacillus Mycobacterium tuberculosis (M.tb), which has evolved to survive inside the hostile
human environment, by avoiding the immune system. Currently, the only licensed vaccine
against TB is the Bacillus Calmette-Guérin (BCG) vaccine, despite the widespread use
it has shown limited protection in adults [2]. New and improved vaccines are urgently
needed and widely researched. Sustained control of a M.tb infection has been linked to
less diﬀerentiated CD4+ T cells with the ability to transmigrate from the vasculature
into the infected tissue, and form populations of both residents and circulating memory
T cells [3]. The subunit vaccine H56/CAF01 (SSI), have been shown to give prolonged
control of infection in mice, associated with central memory T cells (TCMs) [3]. However,
what fully constitutes a protective immune response has not yet been determined.
In this thesis gene expression analysis was used to investigate expression diﬀerences of
H56/CAF01 vs. BCG primed CD4+ T cells. The purpose being to ﬁnd new markers as-
sociated with protection against M.tb infection. The gene expression was performed with
a nCounter SPRINT Proﬁler (Nanostring Technologies), and the sample input was RNA,
puriﬁed from MHC II Tetramer sorted CD4+ memory T cells, and MHC II Tetramer en-
riched CD4+ eﬀector T cells, primed by BCG or H56/CAF01 immunization. Obtaining
RNA from memory cells in suﬃcient quantity and quality showed to be diﬃcult and sev-
eral procedure optimizations were identiﬁed and performed. Especially the performance
of tetramer staining and RNA puriﬁcation was shown to be essential. However, time
did not allow for optimization of ampliﬁcation performance. the result showed that not
enough ampliﬁcation cycles were used, creating a dataset with low gene counts.
BCG primed CD4+ resting memory T cells showed a high activation and thereby dif-
ferentiation state, with higher expression levels of migratory markers compared to the
H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ resting memory T cells. Which instead expressed high levels
of Il7r (CD127), associated with long-lived memory cells and might be TCMs, resting in
the spleen. Some genes which are not typically employed in memory response experi-
ments were found to be candidates for further research. Besides Il7r these included the
migration markers Itgb1 (VLA-1 and VLA-4), and Itgb2/Itgal (LFA-1). The result shows
that a gene expression assay can be used to gain a more detailed view of the response
primed by immunizations. Leading to understanding the factors involved in a protective
response against M.tb
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Dansk Resumé
Tuberkulose (TB) er en smitsom lungesygdom og en af de største dødsårsager i verden, i
det den dræber over en million mennesker årligt [1]. TB er forårsaget af infektion med den
intercellulære bacillus Mycobacterium tuberculosis (M.tb), som har udviklet egenskaben
til at kunne overleve i det fjendtlige menneskelige miljø ved at undgå immunsystemet.
Der er i øjeblikket kun en vaccine i brug mod TB, Bacillus Calmette-Guérin (BCG).
Til trods for den store anvendelse har det vidst sig at den giver begrænset beskyttelse i
voksne [2]. Der er derfor brug for nye og forbedrede vacciner. En vedvarende kontrol af
en M.tb infektion er blevet forbundet med mindre diﬀerentierede CD4+ T celler, der har
evnen til at transmigere fra vaskulaturen til det inﬁcerede væv, og forme populationer
af hukommelseceller [3]. H56/CAF01 vaccinen (SSI) har i mus vist, at kunne lede til en
langvarig kontrol over infektion, denne kontrol er associeret med centrale hukommelses-
celler [3]. Forskning har endnu ikke vidst hvad der udgør en optimal beskyttende immun
respons.
I denne afhandling blev analyse af genudtryk brugt til at undersøge ekspressions forskelle
i H56/CAF01 vs. BCG primet CD4+ T celler, med det formål at ﬁnde nye markører for-
bundet med beskyttelse imod M.tb infektion. Analysen af genudtrykket blev udført af en
nCounter SPRINT Proﬁler (Nanostring Technologies), og prøverne var RNA, oprenset fra
MHC II Tetramer sorteret CD4+ hukommelseceller og MHC II Tetramer beriget CD4+
eﬀector T celler, primet af BCG eller H56/CAF01 immunisering. At få RNA oprenset
fra hukommelsesceller i en tilstrækkelig mængde og kvalitet viste sig at være vanskeligt,
og ﬂere optimeringer måtte identiﬁceres og udføres. Dette vidste sig især at være vigtigt
ved tetramer farvning og RNA-nedbrydning. Projektet tidsrammegav ikke mulighed for
at optimere ampliﬁcationen. Datasættet indehold lave gen tællinger, hvilket vidste at
der ikke blev kørt nok cykler. CD4+ hukommelse T celler primet af BCG viste en høj
aktiverings og dermed diﬀerentieringsstatus med højere ekspressions niveauer af migra-
tionsmarkører sammenlignet med H56/CAF01 primet CD4+ hukommelsesceller. Disse
udtrykte høje niveauer af Il7r (CD127), der er forbundet med centrale hukommelsesceller.
Gener, som ikke typisk anvendes i hukommelse respons forsøg, viste sig at være kandi-
dater til videre undersøgelse. Udover Il7r inkluderede disse migrationsmarkørerne Itgb1
(VLA-1 og VLA-4) og Itgb2/Itgal (LFA-1). De kunne være interessant at undersøge om
udtrykket af disse gener, kan være associeret med bedre beskyttelse. Resultatet viste at
en analyse af genudtryk er en interessant tilgang, til at få et mere detaljeret billede af
responsen efter immuniseringer. Dette kunne blive brugt til at forstå de faktorer, der er
involveret i beskyttelse imod M.tb.

==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Tuberculosis
Tuberculosis (TB) is an infectious disease typically aﬀecting the lungs (pulmonary TB).
However, pathogen dissemination to other locations can occur (extrapulmonary TB),
though depending on the development of the infection in the lungs [4]. The cause of
TB is an infection with the intracellular bacillus Mycobacterium tuberculosis (M.tb).
Common symptoms are coughing with blood, chest pain, shortness of breath, fever and
weight loss. Although the disease is rarely seen in the western part of the world, it is still
a major global health concern as a considerable part of the world’s population is infected
[1]. Once infected ∼5% develop initial active TB (primary TB), the remaining >90% will
be able to control the infection (Figure 1). However, the pathogen is rarely eliminated
but contained by the immune system, leading to a symptomless latent state of infection
(LTBI). Reactivation and development of active TB is observed in 5-10% of LTBI cases
[5]. Reactivation occurs when the immune system is unable to contain the pathogen, a
situation that could arise due to compromised immunity, e.g. co-infection with human
immunodeﬁciency virus (HIV)[6].
Figure 1: Disease Progression. Progression following a Mycobacterium tuberculosis infection. ∼5%
of all infected individuals develop primary disease (Blue), in the remaining cases, the immune system is
capable of controlling the bacteria, leading to latent infection (Green). 5-10% of the latently infected indi-
viduals develop active disease by reactivation (Red), this mostly occurs in individuals with compromised
immune system [5].
==PAGE:21==
2
Introduction
1.1.1
TB Burden
TB causes human mortality globally, being one of the top ten causes of death worldwide.
In 2016, the World Health Organization (WHO) estimated that there were 1.3 million
TB coursed deaths, and additional 374.000 deaths among HIV-coinfected people. The
estimated incidence (number of new and relapse cases) was 10.4 million of these 6.3 million
were new TB cases. The most substantial TB burden is in poor and over populated areas,
whereas the western world is mostly cleared of the disease, with just a few incidents per
year (Figure 2) [1].
Figure 2: Tuberculosis Incidence Rates 2016. WHO estimated Tuberculosis (TB) incidence rates
for 2016.
Countries in dark green have high incident rates, and countries with light green have low
incident rates. High-income countries has generally low incident rates and low-income, overpopulated,
countries have higher incident rates [1].
Eﬀective TB diagnosis and treatment has resulted in a 37% decline in TB mortality rate
in the period 2000-2016, avoiding 53 million TB caused deaths. Despite the progress,
there are still diagnostic and treatment gaps. Health systems miss millions of people
with TB every year and drug resistance increase [1]. One of the most used diagnostic
method is the Tuberculin Skin Test (TST), an intradermal injection of a puriﬁed protein
derivative of tuberculin, a component of M.tb.
If the individual previously has been
exposed to M.tb, the immune system reacts, producing a small, localized reaction, and
this induration is used for diagnosis. However, TST can have diﬃculties distinguishing
between active disease, latent infection, and past vaccination [7].
A newer and more
speciﬁc diagnostic method is the Interferon Gamma Release Assays (IGRAs). IGRAs
can identify exposure to M.tb by measuring the amount of Interferon gamma (IFN-γ)
released from lymphocytes after incubation with antigens expressed exclusively by M.tb
[8]. Although IGRAs are more speciﬁc than TST, they are also more expensive [9]. As
most TB incidence occurs in low-income countries, TST is still widely used.
==PAGE:22==
Introduction
3
The treatment for active TB consists of an extended antibiotic therapy, of which the
ﬁrst two months consist of a intensive phase of rifampicin, isoniazid, pyrazinamide, and
ethambutol followed by four months of only rifampicin and isoniazid [1]. Despite the long
intensive treatment, Multi-Drug Resistant TB and Extensive Drug Resistant TB is still
developing [10]. The time frame and cost of the treatment create factors contributing to
the development of drug resistance, such as low drug quality, incorrect combination, and
failure to complete treatment [11]. Currently, the only licensed vaccine against TB is the
Bacillus Calmette-Guérin (BCG) vaccine, which is composed of an attenuated strain of
Mycobacterium bovis. The BCG vaccine provides limited protection against pulmonary
TB in adolescents, and adults [12]. However, it can prevent severe forms of childhood
TB [1], including TB meningitis, and is therefore widely used [2].
1.1.2
Mycobacterium tuberculosis
M.tb is a member of the genus Mycobacterium, which are weak gram-positive, non-motile,
and non-sporing, shaped as straight or slightly curved rods about 3x0.3µm in size [13].
The mycobacterial cell wall has a complex structure that inﬂuences growth, survival,
resistance to antibiotics, and virulence [14]. The wall consists of three layers and a capsule
of proteins and polysaccharides. The inner layer, surrounding the plasma membrane,
consist of peptidoglycan, the middle layer of arabinogalactan and the outer layer of
mycolic acid - giving it a waxy surface which makes it hydrophobic and resistant to drying
[13]. The thick cell wall impairs the entry of nutrients causing slow growth, nevertheless
increases M.tb resistance to degradation by lysosomal enzymes.
1.2
Immunology
Transmission of M.tb is airborne, and occurs when bacteria are expelled from an individ-
ual with active TB, and thereafter inhaled by a new host. It can occur during exhalation,
coughing or sneezing and distributed in the form of small aerosolized droplets containing
M.tb [15].
1.2.1
Innate immunity
Inhaled M.tb bacilli travels to the pulmonary alveoli, where they are recognized and taken
up by alveolar macrophages, and other phagocytic cells, including dendritic cells, mono-
cytes and neutrophils [13, 16]. The phagocytic cells are activated when their speciﬁc Pat-
tern Recognition Receptors (PRRs) recognize Pathogen-Associated Molecular Patterns
(PAMPs) on the bacterial surface. M.tb PAMPs, such as lipoproteins, are recognized
by several major groups of PRRs including Toll-like receptors (TLRs), Complement Re-
ceptors (CRs), C-type Lectin Receptors (CLRs), and nucleotide-binding-oligomerization-
domain-like receptors [17]. Their activation results in cytokine secretion, which regulates
the innate immune response and initiate the adaptive immune response (Section 1.2.3).
==PAGE:23==
4
Introduction
The alveolar macrophages begin to produce inﬂammatory cytokines; Tumor necrosis
factor-α (TNF-α), and interleukin (IL) 6, 10 and 12, as well as chemokines; C-X-C motif
ligand- (CXCL-) 8 and Monocyte Chemoattractant Protein-1 (MCP-1). This secretion
results in the attraction of monocytes, macrophages, neutrophils, and lymphocytes to the
site of infection. In most infections the recruitment of phagocytes will restrict and elimi-
nate the pathogen. Phagocytic cells engulf the pathogen after recognition, and pack it into
intracellular phagosomes, where they are broken down by fusion with lysosomes contain-
ing a variety of degrading enzymes and antimicrobial substances [18]. However, M.tb can
manipulate the phagocytic cells for their advantage, and the recruitment in early stages
of infection is thereby beneﬁcial for the bacterium as it provides additional host cells for
infection [15]. There are several outcomes for antigen presenting cells (APCs) infected
by a tubercle bacillus (Figure 3). One of the ways M.tb can manipulate the phagocytic
cells is by avoiding phagosomal degradation, by preventing phagosomal maturation and
thereby the fusion of phagosome and lysosome [19]. Without fusion with lysosomes, M.tb
can then begin to proliferate and establish an infection within the phagosomes [20]. M.tb
also posses several virulent mechanisms that optimize their spread from cell to cell. In-
cluding the ESAT-6 Secretion System (ESX)-1 type VII secretion system that promotes
the recruitment of macrophages and necrotic death of infected cells, allowing them to
be released and infect new phagocytes [15]. M.tb express a surface lipid, Phthiocerol
Dimycocerosates, that mask their PAMPs and thus avoid recognition by macrophages
[21]. Resulting in little restriction in bacterial growth during the innate response. When
dendritic cells (DCs) are infected and migrate to the regional lymph node, they can ini-
tiate the activation of an adaptive immune response leading to propagation of T cells,
which can then activate macrophages for more eﬃcient killing of the pathogen [15].
Figure 3:
M.tb Infected Macrophages.
Illustration of the three outcomes for M.tb infected
Macrophages, 1. Lysis of Macrophages leading to mycobacterial spread. 2. Establishment of chronic
infection in the Macrophages leading to latent infection.
3.
M.tb is killed oﬀby T cell activated
macrophages (With modiﬁcations [22]).
==PAGE:24==
Introduction
5
1.2.2
Granuloma Formation
When lymphocytes are recruited to the site of infection, they aggregate around the in-
fected macrophages and extracellular M.tb, forming a structure known as a granuloma.
The presence of granulomas in the lungs are the hallmark of M.tb infection, capable
of "walling oﬀ" the bacteria and establish control of the infection. A granuloma con-
sists of a necrotic central core of infected macrophages and the extracellular M.tb. The
core is encapsulated by a dense cellular wall composed of extracellular matrix, activated
macrophages, neutrophils, DCs, B and T cells, and natural killer cells (NK cells) (Figure
4) [23, 24]. Macrophages can fuse into multinucleated giant cells that appear to have
heightened antimicrobial activity. Cytotoxic T cells and NK cells also contribute to a
protective immunity by inducing apoptosis of the infected macrophages. In large granulo-
mas, the cells in the center become isolated and die due to the cytotoxic eﬀect of activated
macrophages and lack of oxygen. The center is thereby a mix of necrotic tissue and dead
macrophages and is referred to as "caseous necrosis" describing the white and cheese like
appearance and consistency. The tissue that is encapsulated by the granulomas under-
goes ﬁbrosis and calciﬁcation leading to scar tissue visible on X-rays [23]. A granuloma
is usually suﬃcient in limiting the primary infection and can control the infection so it
goes into a latent state. The control is upheld as long as a solid granuloma is maintained,
which is stabilized by the an adaptive immune response. Most mice strains lack structure
and organization of granulomas similar to humans, lesions can be seen, however formed
by loose non-necrotic cellular aggregates with lack of encapsulation [25].
Figure 4: Structure of the Tuberculous Granuloma. The tuberculous granuloma has a core that
consist of infected macrophages and extracellular M.tb . The core is surrounded by macrophages, giant
cells, neutrophils, DCs, B and T cells, and NK cells (with modiﬁcations [24]).
==PAGE:25==
6
Introduction
1.2.3
Adaptive Immunity
The adaptive immunity is initiated through infected DCs migration from the site of
infection to the regional lymph node. This onset is delayed in a M.tb infection, which is
an advantage for the bacteria as it allows the population in the lungs to expand [20]. In
humans, adaptive immune responses arise approximately 42 days after exposure (in mice
it is shown to be 11-14 days after aerosol infection), compared to 20 hours in an inﬂuenza
infection [15]. The mechanism behind this is poorly understood, though one theory is
that M.tb infected DCs have a decreased expression of CC-chemokine Receptor (CCR)
7, which is attracted to the Chemokine ligand (CCL) 21 produced by the lymph nodes.
Interaction between CCR7 and CCL21 facilitates the migration of infected DCs to the
lymph nodes, and a decrease of CCR7 expression could lead to delayed migration [26].
Once in the draining lymph nodes, DCs activate adaptive immune cells to proliferate,
and diﬀerentiate into eﬀector cells, which migrate to the site of infection (Section 1.2.5).
Here they can perform speciﬁc eﬀector functions, in the immune response against M.tb
[15]. In systemic infection, DCs can also reach the spleen were they are attracted to the T
cell zone by a response to CCR7 ligands, and from here they can also initiate an adaptive
immune response [27]. An immune response capable of maintaining control of a M.tb
infection should be strong enough to limit bacterial growth and dissemination, as well as
limit excessive inﬂammation, which could cause host pathology. The response therefore
consist of diﬀerent lymphocyte subsets (Figure 5). After activation the antigen-speciﬁc
T cells express speciﬁc migration receptors enabling them to migrate the site of infection
(Section 1.2.5). A memory response is developed after primary infection to protect the
host for a secondary infection (Section 1.2.6).
CD4+ T cells
An eﬀective adaptive immune response against M.tb infection requires an antigen speciﬁc-
CD4+ T cell response [15]. Naive CD4+ T cells are activated in the lymph node, when
their T cell receptors (TCRs) interact with antigen, loaded on Major Histocompatibility
Complex (MHC) class II molecules on the surface of DC [5]. Besides antigen recogni-
tion, the activation also depends on co-stimulatory signals and a cytokine proﬁle, which
determines the diﬀerentiation towards distinct eﬀector T cells subsets as well as the ex-
pression of migration properties. The activation introduce a signaling cascade within the
T cell, leading to gene transcription promoting T cell expansion and diﬀerentiation [28].
The precise composition of the T cell subsets needed for protection against M.tb is still
unclear. However, it has been clearly established that Type 1 T helper cells (TH1) are
essential for control [4]. Nevertheless, individuals with a strong TH1 response can still
progress disease, leading to the perception that the protective T cell response is more
complex and needs to be composed of more than one type.
==PAGE:26==
Introduction
7
Figure 5: Adaptive Immune Response against M.tb. Overview of the adaptive immune response
against M.tb. M.tb survives inside macrophages (Mφ) and dendritic cells (DCs), antigens are presented
to CD-4+ T cell by MHC class II molecules.
CD4+ T helper cells proliferate into diﬀerent subsets;
TH1, TH17, Tregs, with each their eﬀector function. CD8+ T cells are activated through MHC Class I
and protect by the production of IFN-γ and lysis of M.tb infected APCs. The response can control the
infection within a solid granuloma, leading to latent infection. If the response is insuﬃcient, it can lead
to active disease, when the bacteria is no longer encapsulated inside a granuloma [29].
Type 1 T helper cells
TH1 polarization is initiated through IL-12 produced by the activating DCs [20], IL-12
induces expression of the transcription factor T-bet enabling IFN-γ production and TH1
lineage commitment [28]. TH1 produce IL-2 for further T cell activation, and IFN-γ and
TNF for macrophage activation [29, 20]. Deteriorated disease manifestations have been
observed in humans carrying mutations in IFN-γ and IL-2 signaling pathways [30], and
after treatment with anti-TNF [31, 4], validating the importance of a TH1 response. M.tb
speciﬁc TH1 cells activate infected macrophages by recognition of M.tb antigen presented
on MHC class II molecules, and their secretion of IFN-γ, and expression of CD40 ligand.
The activation of macrophages enables the fusion of phagosomes with the lysosomes,
leading to enhanced killing. The activated macrophages start to express higher levels
of B7 molecules (CD80/CD86), CD40, MHC class II molecules and TNF receptors on
its surface, which will increase the activation of T cells.
Macrophages secrete IL-12,
which increases TH1 polarization. IFN-γ responses from CD4+ T cells are known to be
essential for control of M.tb infection, based on studies in IFN-γ knock out mice, which
quickly succumb to M.tb infection and the previously mentioned human studies with
IFN-γ mutation [32]. However, it has recently been discussed if IFN-γ signaling pathway
may not be essential for CD4+ T cell-mediated protective immunity against M.tb.
==PAGE:27==
8
Introduction
This arises from the diﬃculties in correlating the ability of vaccines to induce IFN-γ
producing TH1 cells with protection against M.tb. Instead, it has been suggested that
TH1 primed CD4+ T cells have mechanisms to protect against TB independently of IFN-
γ production. What cytokines and mechanisms that they can mediate protection against
M.tb, is still unknown [28, 32].
TH17 and Tregs
Type 17 helper T cells (TH17) have shown to improve M.tb control in animal models
[3]. The TH17 phenotype is promoted by IL-23, IL-6, and Transforming Growth Factor
β (TGF-β) secretion, inducing expression of transcription factor RORγt and RORα [28].
TH17 secrete IL-17 that recruit neutrophils and induce the production of local inﬂam-
matory cytokines and chemokines, IL-17 thereby counteract the mycobacterial induced
IL-10 improving control of infection in the absence of TH1 [3]. It has been diﬃcult to
determine the signiﬁcance of TH 17response during a M.tb infection. In animal studies,
TH17 responses are found to be most signiﬁcant early in infection, where they induce
chemokines and facilitate protective CD4+ T cells to the lungs [28], and it has also been
implications that interaction between TH1 and TH17 cells can enhance control of infec-
tion [3]. The immune response needs to be regulated to avoid excessive inﬂammation;
this regulation is conducted by CD4+FOXP3+ regulatory T cells (Tregs). Tregs devel-
opment depends on TGF-β and the transcription factor FOXP3, found at high levels in
humans with active TB. They secrete IL-10 that can inhibit the ability of APCs to ac-
tivate TH1 and the production of pro-inﬂammatory cytokines [4]. They thereby prevent
excessive inﬂammation, but also impose a ceiling on the eﬃcacy of eﬀector T cells that
might lead to higher bacterial burden [20]. The functions of TH17 T cells and Tregs are
opposite, and maintaining the right balance between the two subsets appear critical for
the immunopathogenesis of active TB.
Other Lymphocytes
CD8+ T cells have also shown to contribute to protection against progressive M.tb in-
fection, but the signiﬁcance in humans has not yet been determined [20]. Primed by the
presentation of antigen onto MHC class I molecules, CD8+ T cells will express Fas ligand
and produce IFN-γ, perforin, granzymes, and granulysin, which can lead to lysis of M.tb
infected APCs. Even though M.tb is an intracellular pathogen, it had recently been re-
vised that B cell/antibodies aﬀect protection against M.tb infection. Passive transfer of
antibodies has been shown to increase protection, and deﬁciencies in antibody production
to be associated with increased susceptibility. Antibody-mediated protection could be
achieved through promotion of opsonization through Fc receptors (leading to phagocy-
tosis) or by activation of complement compounds, which also lead to opsonization and
further cellular recruitment to the site of infection [33].
==PAGE:28==
Introduction
9
1.2.4
Migration
The capacity of T cells to circulate the body is crucial for an eﬀective adaptive immune
response. The ability to traﬃc between lymphoid and non-lymphoid tissues is associated
with activation, eﬃcient eﬀector function and protective memory formation [27]. M.tb
speciﬁc T cells can diﬀer in their expression of several phenotypic markers, including
markers essential for migration.
Migration of naïve T cells from the thymus to the
Lymph Nodes (LNs) or other Secondary Lymphoid Organs (SLOs), is modulated by the
expression of L-selectin (CD62L), P-Selectin Glycoprotein Ligand-1 (PSGL-1), CCR7,
and Lymphocyte function-associated antigen 1 (LFA-1,αLβ2). These receptors interact
with their ligands in the High Endothelial Venules (HEV) of SLOs enabling them to cross
into the parenchyma of secondary lymphoid tissues (Figure 6) [34].
Figure 6: T cell entry to Secondary Lymphoid Organs. T cell rolling in the High Endothelial
Venules (HEVs) is initiated by interaction between T cell receptors: CD26L and PSGL-1, and their
ligands; PNAd and P-selectin.
When T cells encounter CCL21 with CCR7, LFA-2 is activated and
binds to ICAM-1 enabling diapedesis through the HEVs. PNAd: Peripheral lymph Node Addressin, LFA-1:
Lymphocyte Function-Associated antigen-1, ICAM-1: Intercellular Adhesion Molecule-1 (With modiﬁcations
[35]).
Once in the LN, naïve T cells encounter infected DCs and starts to up-regulate expression
of activation markers CD69, CD25 and CD44 [27]. When the T cells are activated, they
start to alter their expression of migration receptors (MRs) and thereby their patterns of
migration [34]. The ﬁnal combination of MR expressed can determine how well the T cells
proliferate and their tissue-speciﬁc migration [27]. This alteration is determined by the
content of peptide, co-stimulatory molecules, and transcription factors during activation.
The co-stimulatory-signals CD28 and Inducible T cell COStimulator (ICOS), promotes
migration out of LN by initiating mechanism leading to the downregulation of CCR7
and CD62L and upregulation of Sphingosine-1-Phosphate receptor 1 (S1P1). S1P1 are
attracted to S1P in lymph leading to migration out of LNs and entry into circulation
[27]. Cytotoxic T Lymphocyte-Associated protein 4 (CTLA-4) that bind to B7 with a
higher binding aﬃnity, it thereby competing with CD28. However, CD28 is constitutively
expressed on T cells, whereas CTLA-4 expression is only induced when the T cells are
activated, in order to avoid overstimulation [27, 36].
==PAGE:29==
10
Introduction
When the T cells are in the circulation, they are directed to the sites of inﬂammation by
the activated endothelium [27]. Endothelial cell is activated by proinﬂammatory cytokines
such as TNF-α, IL-1, and IL-6, and when activated they begin to express selectins (P-
selectin, E-selectin), and Intercellular adhesion molecules (ICAMs). The T cells express
receptors that can bind to the ligands on the endothelial cells. These receptors include
LFA-1 and Very Late Activation antigen 1 (VLA-1, α1β1) and 4 (VLA-4, α4β1). The
latter are important in eﬀector T cell migration as it is expressed late stage of T cell
activation. The interaction between the MRs on T cells and their ligands on endothelial
cells enable T cell migration into the tissues. Expression of distinct MR combination can
lead to tissue-speciﬁc migration. T cells primed in gut-associated lymphoid tissue express
PSGL-1, α4β7, and CCR7. The two latter are driven by rhetoric acid synthesized from
gut-associated DCs, leading to migration to gut tissue. T cells primed in skin-draining
LN migrate to the skin by expression of cutaneous lymphocyte antigen, which binds to
E-selectin on the epithelium of inﬂamed skin. MRs for lung migration is not as identiﬁed,
yet CCR4 have been associated with lung-homing advantages of lung DC activated CD4+
T cells, and many lymphocytes in the lungs express high levels of VLA-4, VLA-1, and
LFA-1. Migration can also be impacted by changes in the site of infection. It has been
demonstrated that activated eﬀector T cells, within chronically inﬂamed M.tb infected
tissues, express high levels of α4β1, regulated by the presence of nitric oxide released
by macrophages, which are highly present in the lungs during M.tb infection [27]. In
conclusion, the function and location of eﬀector T cells are governed by the expression of
diﬀerent MR proﬁles that are regulated by the environment.
1.2.5
Protective phenotypes in relation to lung-homing capacity
Eﬀorts have been put into identifying the fully protective nature of CD4+ T cell responses
against M.tb infection.
The precise nature and phenotype varies elusive, and it has
been anticipated that it is several mechanisms that contribute to the optimal protective
capacity of M.tb-speciﬁc CD4+ T cells rather than one single marker. Mice models have
shown that direct contact between CD4+ T cells and infected APCs lead to better control
of infection, which require that the T cells are located within the lung parenchyma, close
to the infected cells [37]. A key feature of T cells during a M.tb infection is, therefore, to be
able to exit circulation and enter the infected lung parenchyma. To study the distribution
of these various T cell phenotypes, they can be in vivo labeled with intravenously injected
ﬂuorescent antibodies just prior to euthanization.
This technique has identiﬁed that
T cells in the lung parenchyma and those in the lung vasculature represent distinct
populations (Figure 7) [3]. TH1 T cells localized in the lung parenchyma express high
amounts of Programmed cell Death-1 (PD-1), CD69, and ICOS, they have low levels
of Killer Cell Lectin-like Receptor subfamily G member 1 (KLRG1) and CD62L, and
intermediate levels of T-bet, consistent with an activated CD4+ T cell of intermediary
diﬀerentiation [3, 12].
==PAGE:30==
Introduction
11
Figure 7: Homing Properties of M.tb speciﬁc CD4+ T cells. TH1 has two major phenotypes,
lung parenchymal TH1 cells and lung intravascular TH1 cells. They diﬀer in their migration properties,
functions and host protective capacity (With corrections [32]).
CD4+ T cells retained in lung vasculature express high levels of KLRG1 and high T-
bet expression associated with terminally diﬀerentiated eﬀector T cells producing IFN-γ
with a high eﬃciency [38]. PD-1 is a cell surface receptor expressed on activated T cells.
When bound to either of its two ligands (PD-L1/PD-L2) on APCs, PD-1 regulates T
cell function by inhibiting cytokine production and proliferation [39]. Nevertheless, mice
lacking PD-1 have shown to be highly sensitive to M.tb infection, due to excessive im-
mune pathology [40]. The signals from PD-1 may increase the TCR threshold for antigen
stimulation and thereby increase the level of TCR signaling needed for activation, thus
counteracting T cell exhaustion and terminally diﬀerentiation. This could prevent ex-
cessive T cell activation, and associated pathology, and lead to sustained protection, by
maintaining TH1 response throughout chronic stages of infection [41]. PD-1+ TH1 T cell
have memory like properties, such as survival in the absence of antigen and the ability to
mount a robust secondary response [41]. KLRG1 is an inhibitory receptor on T cells, with
increased expression on older T cells, making it a marker of terminally diﬀerentiated and
short lived T cells [38]. Lack of KLRG1 in mice have showed improved survival, reduced
bacterial burden, and enhanced activation as well as enhanced eﬀector function of CD4+
T cells during a M.tb infection [42]. However, has been suggested that during a M.tb
infection PD1+ TH1 T cells can undergo a phenotypic transition to more diﬀerentiated
KLRG1+ eﬀector TH1 T cells [38].
The two populations are further distinguished by their expression of chemokine recep-
tors, which inﬂuences their migratory pattern.
Parenchymal T cells express (C-X-C
motif) Receptor- (CXCR-) 3, involved in migration to the lung parenchyma by binding
to it ligands CXCL9-11 expressed in inﬂammed tissue. Intravascular T cells express the
vascular homing cytokine C-X3-C motif ligand- (CX3CR-) 1, which binds the fractaline
(CX3CL1) either in soluble form or bound to activated endothelial cells. Neither may
play an essential role for control, although, they aﬀect the ability to transmigrate into
the parenchymal tissue [12].
==PAGE:31==
12
Introduction
Adoptive transfer experiments showed that KLRG1−CXCR3+ T cell administered to the
vasculature, were better than the KLRG1+CX3CR1+ cells in re-traﬃcking back into the
lung parenchyma and in mediating bacterial control [3, 12]. The ability to exit and re-
enter the lungs can potentially relieve the T cells for terminal diﬀerentiation as they can
take a "break" from the intense inﬂammatory environment within the lungs [3]. Less
diﬀerentiated parenchymal CD4+ T cells, have shown to mediate greater control of M.tb
infection, so the ability to transmigrate from the vasculature and enter the infected tissue
correlates with improved protection [12, 3, 43]. Parenchyma cell produces low levels of
TH1 eﬀector cytokines such as IFN-γ, but they produce high levels of IL-2 [43]. The
intravascular counterparts produce high levels of IFN-γ, control of M.tb may, therefore,
correlate better with the ability to enter the lung parenchyma and IL-2 production than
with high IFN-γ production per se. Parenchyma cells produce some IFN-γ, probably
enough to mediate bacterial control, without promoting excessive inﬂammation or ter-
minal exhaustion [12].
1.2.6
Post-primary infection
Although necessary, adaptive immunity is rarely suﬃcient in fully eradicating the bac-
teria. Insuﬃcient eradication establishes a chronic infection, where a continuous T cell
response is needed to contain M.tb [12]. Animal models and data from infected indi-
viduals have shown that chronic TB infection can drive CD8+T cells into a state of
terminal diﬀerentiation, leading to impaired T cell function [2, 38]. The same concept is
not equally well understood concerning CD4+ T cells, but there is a clear indication that
immune regulation and functional exhaustion occur in chronic infections.
Memory T cells
After eradication of a pathogen in infection or clearance of antigen in immunization, most
eﬀector T cells (up to 95%) undergo apoptosis, due to the absence of stimuli. After the
contraction, this leave behind a pool of heterogeneous long-lived memory cells [44, 45].
Though, a protective memory response are not seen after a M.tb infection [43]. Memory T
cells are superior in migration and activation compared to naive T cells. TCRs on memory
T cells are more sensitive, and the activation requires less co-stimulatory signaling. They
can perform immunosurveillance as they are able of entering either SLOs or peripheral
tissues. In case of a secondary infection, the memory T cells are capable of initiating a
rapid immune response much quicker than during a primary infection [46]. The memory
T cell concept was ﬁrst described in humans, but mice studies have shown that mouse
and human memory T cell population are fundamentally similar [34]. CD8+ memory T
cells and memory B cells are thought to play less signiﬁcant role than CD4+ memory T
cells for M.tb protection, and the following is concerning CD4+ memory T cells. However,
it should be taken into account that most T cell memory studies, have been performed
on CD8+ memory T cells, and diﬀerences are not fully determined yet [28].
==PAGE:32==
Introduction
13
Eﬀector T cells are during chronic infections exposed to antigens for a prolonged time.
Such an environment drive T cells to exhaustion, resulting in the generation of short-
lived eﬀector T cells rather than long-lived memory T cells. This is the case during a
TB infection or after BCG immunization, as it consists of a live organisms that persist
[28]. On the other hand, a low antigen exposure can lead to an insuﬃcient generation
of memory T cells. Generating a suﬃcient immunological memory response against M.tb
should, therefore, be able to provide the right environment, that balance the exposure of
antigens [28]. The mechanisms behind development of a memory T cell are still not fully
understood, though diﬀerent models has been proposed; The linear model, the progressive
model or the divergent model. The linear model suggests that memory T cells are directly
generated from eﬀector T cells. The progressive diﬀerentiation model states that it is the
amplitude of the activation of the naive T cells that decide the fate. Highly activated T
cells become short-lived eﬀector cells, and less activated T cells become memory T cells,
with an ability to further diﬀerentiate. The last model, the divergent model, suggest
that the fate is decided right after activation, and inﬂuenced by diﬀerent signals present
[28]. Memory T cells can be divided into diﬀerent subsets [44]; Central Memory T cells
(TCM), Eﬀector Memory T cells (TEM), and Tissue-Resident Memory T cells (TRM)
(Figure 8).
Figure 8: Memory T cells. TCM Recirculate through secondary lymhoid organs (Spleen and lym-
phnodes), TEM recirculate more through the spleen, than the lymph nodes. Signiﬁcant numbers of TEM
migrate to the lungs and return to circulation through lymphatic vessels. TRM localize within previously
challenged lung tissue (With modiﬁcations [34, 28]).
==PAGE:33==
14
Introduction
TCMs are located in the SLOs, due to their expression of CCR7 and CD62L [44]. They
are also CD127+, CD44hi, produce IL-2 and can proliferate extensively. They can self-
renew independently of antigen stimulation, and their proliferation rate is slow when not
activated making them long-lived. Activated TCMs rapidly generate secondary eﬀector
T cells that can migrate to the site of infection. TEMs lack expression of the SLO homing
molecules CCR7 and CD62L instead they can circulate the blood and peripheral tissue
[34]. Which tissues it can migrate to, are determined by expression of tissue homing re-
ceptors [44]. TEMs are CD44hi and express CD127, and does not proliferate to the same
extent as TCMs, but produce eﬀector cytokines such as IFN-γ, IL-2, IL-17 and TNF-α
[28]. TEMs rely on antigen stimulation for survival and proliferate at a high rate and are
therefore shorter lived than TCMs. For an eﬃcient protective memory response TEMs
are essential as they can perform immunosurveillance and react quickly on reinfection.
However as they have a short-lived lifespan, TCMs are necessary to establish long-term
memory responses. TRMs lack CCR7 and CD62L, and do not circulate. Instead, they
reside in the aﬀected tissue after cleared infection [34]. Tissue resident memory T cells
can when activated, result in rapid production of eﬀector chemokines and cytokines, and
initiate an innate immune response [46]. TRMs are CD44hiCD11ahi and express CD69
and CD103 [34]. They also express the same homing markers; PD-1 and CXCR3, as the
protective parenchymal TH1 T cells, and might be generated from these after cleared
infection [28]. It has been suggested that polyfunctional CD4 T cells that can produce
multiple pro-inﬂammatory cytokines correlate with protection. In a vaccine setting in
mice "triple positive" eﬀector memory T cells secreting IFN-γ+TNF-α+IL-2+ and "dou-
ble positive" central memory TNF-α+IL-2+ have gained signiﬁcant interest as they are
seen to induce long-term memory [47]. However, this correlation has been questioned
as polyfunctional T cells have not shown to be suﬃcient enough to mediate a protective
memory response to M.tb [28], and therefore may not be necessary for protection. This
indicates that a protective response might depend on several factors such as additional
eﬀector functions, tissue homing, and long-term survival capacity [48].
1.3
Vaccine Strategies
WHO has established an "End TB" Strategy with the aim to end the global TB epidemic,
by reducing TB deaths by 90% and new cases with 80% by 2030 [1, 6]. With the lack of
an eﬀective vaccine in adults, vaccine research and development has become an essential
part of this strategy. A vaccine needs to manage the obstacles M.tb has provided for the
immune system and be able to maintain a strong memory immunity that can prevent
infection or provide long-term containment of infection, in an environment with continu-
ously exposure to antigen [2]. Currently, the understanding is that such a vaccine should
introduce a strong TH1 and T cell response and TCMs/TRMs memory response [32], with
high ability to migrate to the lungs [3].
==PAGE:34==
Introduction
15
1.3.1
BCG
The BCG vaccine was developed between 1906 and 1919, where the Mycobacterium bovis
bacterium was attenuated through several laboratory passages, until it no longer induced
disease in lab animals [49, 50]. The underlying avirulent version of the attenuated BCG
strain was later found by comparative genomics studies with the virulent M.tb laboratory
strain H37Rv.
These studies reviled a deletion of genes in chromosomal Regions of
Diﬀerence 1 (RD1) in BCG. RD1 encodes for the ESX-1 secretion system and the deletions
was a cluster of six genes (Rv3871-Rv3876) from the locus of the ESX-1 secreted ESAT-6
antigen [15, 51]. BCG vaccine was ﬁrst used in humans in 1921 [29], and have been
used ever since, becoming the most widely used vaccine world wide. It is however mostly
administered in children [28], as it show high eﬃcacy against childhood TB meningitis and
miliary disease. However, the protective eﬃcacy against pulmonary disease is variable,
raging from 0-80%, and have an average duration of protection for about 10 years [52].
Mice model studies have shown that immunization with BCG induces a protective TH1
immune response that can control M.tb growth for the ﬁrst 6-10 weeks of infection,
however when the infection enters late chronic stages, the protection is gradually lost.
The loss of protection is associated with a decrease in the number of IL-2-producing
CD4+ T cells and an increase in terminally diﬀerentiated KLRG1+ T cells, leading to
less TCMs memory T cells [2].
1.3.2
Novel TB vaccines
Due to the ineﬀectiveness of BCG, several novel TB vaccine candidates have been devel-
oped. An eﬀective vaccine strategy should consist of three types of vaccines; A vaccine
that initially can prime the immune system, A vaccine that can boost such vaccine, and
a post-exposure vaccine that target latent M.tb and prevent reactivation [29]. Targeting
latent infection is done either by boosting an existing response, from prior exposure to
M.tb or a BCG vaccination, or administered together with the standard TB treatment,
to improve the immune response and prevent the development of recurrent disease [29].
There are currently 12 vaccine candidates that have reached clinical trials (Figure 9).
Eight of the candidates are subunit vaccines, based on M.tb antigens, that can either be
recombinant fusion proteins (eg. H56:IC31) or viral vectors that express antigens (e.g
Ad5 Ag85b). Subunit vaccines are composed of antigens expressed by the pathogen, often
secreted antigens, as they are very accessible to the immune cells during an infection and
are therefore critical for recognition. A subunit vaccine can be designed as a multistage
vaccine by combining antigens that are expressed at diﬀerent stages of the infection.
Subunit vaccines often need to be administered several times and be accompanied by an
adjuvant to be able to promote a response. The adjuvant should be selected to drive the
desired type of response.
==PAGE:35==
16
Introduction
Figure 9: Global Clinical Pipeline of TB vaccine Candidates. Stages for TB vaccines Candidates
as of September 2017 as reported by the vaccine sponsors. (Aeras)
Adjuvant CAF01
Cationic Adjuvant Formulation 01 (CAF01) is a liposomal adjuvant developed by Statens
Serum Institute. The liposome is formed by the quaternary ammonium compound N,N’-
dimethyl-N,N’-dioctadecylammonium (DDA) stabilized by the synthetic mycobacterial
immunostimulant α, α′-trehalose 6,6’-dibeheneate (TDB) by insertion into the lipid bi-
layers [53]. DDA assembles into the liposome-like form since it is a synthetic amphiphilic
lipid with a hydrophilic positively charged head and two hydrophobic tails. In an aqueous
solution the tails will turn away from the hydrophilic environment and create a hydropho-
bic bilayer. TDB is the main immunomodulatory component of CAF01, as it activates
APCs enabling the adjuvant to induce a strong cellular immune response and antibody
response.
TDB is a synthetic analog of trehalose 6,6’-dimycolate (TDM), which is a
naturally occurring immunostimulatory component of the mycobacterial cell wall. How-
ever, TDB has a lower toxicity than TDM. CAF01 is a TH1/TH17- promoting adjuvant
[54], releasing high levels of IFN-y/IL-2 and IL-17A respectively. For use in TB vaccine,
antigens can be electrostatically absorbed onto the CAF01 liposomes. At the site of in-
fection, CAF01 will generate a depot eﬀect and mediate a strong interactions with APCs,
resulting in continuous but low activation of APCs [53].
H56/CAF01
Several TB subunit vaccine candidates are currently tested, one of them is the Hybrid56
(H56) vaccine from SSI, currently entering clinical phase II. H56 is a subunit vaccine
consisting of a CAF01-adjuvant fusion protein of M.tb antigens Ag85B, ESAT-6, and
Rv2660. These antigens are expressed by M.tb during an infection. Ag85B is an early
secreted antigen and ESAT-6 is continually expressed throughout M.tb infection and
primes a strong T cell response in infected mice [55]. Rv2660 is a late expressed antigen
and preferentially expressed during latent infection. H56/CAF01 have been shown to
==PAGE:36==
Introduction
17
give prolonged control of infection in mice, associated with TCMs memory T cells [3].
Immunization-primed ESAT-6 speciﬁc T cells have a non terminally diﬀerentiated pheno-
type with no or limited KLRG1 and increased CXCR3 expression, as well as expression of
IL-2 and IL-17A [43]. They can be found circulating with an enhanced ability to localize
to the lung parenchyma [3, 2]. M.tb speciﬁc T cells primed by H56 exhibit a diﬀerent
phenotype than their infection-primed counterparts. A BCG and M.tb infection provide
sustained antigen availability and inﬂammatory signaling, leading to a phenotype of fully
diﬀerentiated TH1 cells with high T-bet and KLRG1 expression [43]. This phenotype
has poor ability to home into the lung parenchyma. Compared to BCG and previously
infection, the H56/CAF01 vaccine has shown to give signiﬁcantly better protection at
week 12 and 24 post infection in mice. Measured by the colony forming units (CFU)
in infected lungs [56]. Memory T cells generated after infection were shown to be more
diﬀerentiated than those primed by an H56/CAF01, giving the subunit vaccine a supe-
rior capacity to home infected lung parenchyma and maintaining a long-term response.
H56/CAF01 used as a BCG boost vaccine improved protection at week 24 after infection,
compared to mice receiving only BCG. In a post-exposure setting, mice where infected
and cleared by antimicrobial drugs before vaccination. Mice vaccinated with H56/CAF01
where more protected against regrowth/reinfection than non-vaccinated mice.
1.4
Animal models
Speciﬁc aspects of TB biology, such as bacterial killing and detailed immunological re-
sponse, are not possible to test in vivo in humans. The use of animal models enables this
as they create reproducible in vivo manipulation systems [4]. However, animals models
have limitations regarding interpretations of the results for use in human vaccine studies,
and no animal model fully reproduce the TB disease proﬁle we see in humans [57].
1.4.1
Mouse model
The mouse model is the most commonly used animal model in TB research [58]. Mice
are small, easy to hold, cost-eﬀective, have high reproducibility, and there are numerous
immunological tool and reagents available [24]. Research with the mouse model have
provided valuable information about the immune response against M.tb, such as the im-
portance of CD4+ T cells, especially the TH1 pathway, and TLR signaling [59]. BCG
vaccination in mice give great protection against aerosol challenge, making the mouse
model important in investigating new TB vaccines. However, there are several limita-
tions within this model. Mice do not develop a classic TB disease as seen in humans,
where the disease is seen in many forms with the most frequent outcome to be a la-
tent infection. Instead, mice develop a chronic, progressive infection of the lungs with
a high bacterial burden, and they do not form well deﬁned granulomas as seen in hu-
mans [58, 57]. Diﬀerent mice strains have shown to have diﬀerent responses to infection.
==PAGE:37==
18
Introduction
The two commonly used strains C57BL/6 and BALB/c fails to develop necrotic lesions.
Whereas the C3HeB/FeJ strain develop necrotic lesions more similar to humans, but
with a severe and fatal infection distinct from human infection [57]. The MHC genes
are highly polymorphic. However, as inbred mice have nearly identical genotype within
a strain, it limits the diversity in the types of MHC molecules, which will inﬂuence the
antigens that can be recognized. C57BC/6 mice have the MHC class II haplotype I-Ab,
and BALB/c mice I-Ad. In choosing a mouse strain for vaccine experiments, it is essen-
tial to select a strain that expresses the MHC haplotype that the antigen is restricted to.
The two TB epitopes ESAT-64−17 and TB10.473−88 are restricted to two diﬀerent MHC
haplotypes; ESAT-64−17 is I-Ab restricted and TB10.473−88 is I-Ad restricted. Neither
of the two strains can thereby be used for an experiment using both antigens, but hybrid
mice that are an oﬀspring between these two strains will express MHC class II haplotypes
(I-Ab/I-Ab) and can thereby be used. In most mice models only female mice are used, as
male mice housed in groups tend to engage in frequent ﬁghting with each other. There is
no diﬀerence in the manifestation of TB between female and male mice, and there have
to this point not been found any diﬀerence in the vaccine take; however it cannot be
ruled out. These limitations provides the question of whether vaccines working in mice
will work in humans for the long-term [59].
1.4.2
Other Animal Models
Another animal model to consider is the guinea pig model, as they have close similarity in
the course of clinical infection to humans and forms human-like granulomas. Guinea pigs
are therefore better in simulating diﬀerent forms of TB and to evaluate protection from
novel vaccines [58]. However, this is not a widely used model as they are expensive and
challenging to use, due to the few immunological tools available [58]. Nonhuman primates
(NHPs) are more closely related to humans, and a more human-like infection will occur,
including phenotypical appearance of granulomas. NHPs would, therefore, provide the
excellent representation of human disease, but such a model is even more costly and more
challenging to manage [58]. Nevertheless, recent experiments with primary M.tb infection
in rhesus macaques shows that all CD4+ T cells found in the lungs were similar to the
parenchymal CD4+ T cell phenotype found in mice, whereas the vascular phenotype
was not present.
There was an indication that the CD4+ T cell position within the
granulomas might be more important than the migration into the parenchyma per se
[60]. This aspect would be diﬃcult study and investigate further in the mouse model
as they do not form well articulated and necrotising granulomas. Currently, no single
animal model is ideal for representing human disease, but as NHPs are closer related to
humans, eﬀort should be made to further develop the NHP model. Not as a replacement
but as a tool to test vaccine candidates before they enter clinical testing.
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2
Aim and Objectives
The aim of this thesis is to identify diﬀerences in gene expression of memory and eﬀector
CD4+ T cells primed by BCG vaccine vs.
H56/CAF01 vaccine.
The purpose is to
gain a better understanding of the CD4+ T cell phenotypes that correlate with vaccine-
elicited protection and thereby what constitutes a protective, nonpathogenic CD4+ T
cell response. On order to provide a rationale for the development and evaluation of
novel M.tb vaccines. Studies have indicated enhanced vaccine-elicited protection against
M.tb correlates with less terminally diﬀerentiated cells, the ability to home infected lung
parenchyma and form populations of both residents and circulating memory T cells.
However, a full understanding of all the mechanisms employed by CD4+ T cells which
mediate protection, are still not determined.
Investigating expression proﬁles can provide insight into the cellular process, which can
contribute to a better understanding of the factors involved in CD4+ T cell mediated
protection. BCG can initiate a protective response but have shown to be incapable of
sustaining long-term protection, whereas the H56/CAF01 subunit vaccine mediates a less
diﬀerentiated response with better homing abilities. By comparing the gene expressions
of the CD4+ T cells primed by these two vaccines, the goal is to gain a broader insight
into their diﬀerences, which can be used to identify new markers that correlate with pro-
tection against a M.tb infection.
The overall objective of this thesis were to:
1. Design an experimental procedure for gene expression analysis, including obtaining
suﬃcient quantity and quality of nucleic acid from the small memory pool of vaccine-
primed CD4+ T cells.
2. Investigate diﬀerences between phenotypes of BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed resting
memory CD4+ T cells.
3. Investigate diﬀerences between phenotypes of BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed sec-
ondary eﬀector CD4+ T cells.
4. Identify possible new markers for vaccine primed CD4+ T cell associated protection
against M.tb.
==PAGE:39==
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3
Experimental Procedures
3.1
Experiments
Two experiments where conducted; F2410 (Figure 10) and F2427 (Figure 11).
Both
experiments consisted of 36 mice in total divided into three groups. One group were
immunized with BCG, another with H56/CAF01, while the third group were a control
group. A third experiment was performed with infected mice from an ongoing memory
experiment (F2340), with similar immunizations (Figure 12). Example of protocols for
animal experiments is found in Appendix A. As problems arose during the experimental
work, F2410 was used to optimize procedures (Chapter 4) to solve these problems and
be able to perform full procedures in F2427.

3.1.1
Resume of Procedure
Experimental work were conducted to obtain puriﬁed RNA from MHC II Tetramer sorted
CD4+ T cells for gene expression analysis. Lymphocytes were isolated (Section 3.2.3)
and stained with appropriate MHC class II tetramers to perform magnetic bead-based
pull-down (Section 3.3). Tetramer positive T cells from non-infected mice were sorted
(Section 3.5). Before RNA puriﬁcation was performed, RNA from infected mice were
puriﬁed directly after dual pull-down (Section 3.6). RNA was converted into cDNA and
multi-target enrichment was performed before samples were sent to be hybridized and
analyzed by Nanostring Technology (Section 3.7). Reagents can be seen in Table 2 and
detailed procedures in Appendix B.

Experimental Procedures
23
3.2
Animal immunizations and infection
Eight weeks old Female CB6F1 mice (CB6F1/OlaHSd mice) were purchased from Envigo
[62]. The mice were kept in the animal facilities at Statens Serum Institut (Copenhagen,
Denmark), where they were handled by authorized personnel, to ensure a consistent,
optimal and humane treatment. Which secured maximum comfort and minimal stress
to the animals. After arrival, the mice were left to rest for one week before initiation of
experiments. During experiments they were fed with radiation-sterilized food and water
ad libitum. In agreement with the Danish Welfare act, all animal experimental protocols
were reviewed by an independent ethical review at Statens Serum Institut, following
an approval that was in accordance with the license for animal experiments issued by
The Animal Experiments Inspectorate under the Ministry of Environmental and Food of
Denmark (License no 2014-15-2934-01065).
3.2.1
Vaccine Formulation and Administration
All mice were immunized subcutaneously (s.c) at the base of the tail, with a volume of
200µL. Immunizations were done either once with BCG vaccine (Group C) or three times
at two-week intervals with the H56/CAF01 vaccine (Group B). The control group (Group
A) were injected with saline at the same time points as H56/CAF01 immunizations (Table
3). BCG vaccinated mice received a dose of 0.5X106 CFU of BCG Danish strain 1331
(Statens Serum Institute, DK) in PBS, to the total volume of 200µL. The bacterium
was grown to log phase at 37◦C and stored at -80◦C in growth medium. During vaccine
preparation, BCG bacteria where defrosted and placed in an ultrasound bath for 5 min for
sonication, followed by homogenization through a suction needle before being diluted in
PBS. To verify inocolum of BCG CFUs, dilutions where plated and incubated for 2 weeks
(Appendix B.1). H56 vaccine dose consisted of 5µg H56 antigen (Batch: D-F0812-01,
Department of Vaccine Development, Statens Serum Institute) and 100µL CAF01 (dosis:
250µg DDA (Acros Organics) and 50µg TDB (Clauson-Kaas)) in Tris Buﬀer (Table 2).
Total vaccine volume was 200µL.

three times at two-week intervals (2w). Group C where immunized once

==PAGE:43==
24
Experimental Procedures
3.2.2
Mycobacterial Challenge
Mice for infection studies were challenged with M.tb Erdman ATCC stain with a concen-
tration of 0.5 x 106 CFU/ml. Infection was done by the aerosol route, using an inhalation
exposure system (Biaera Aero MP aerosol system) that deliver ∼100 CFU per mouse.
All handling with the bacterium and infected mice/organs was performed at Biosafety
level III (BSL-III) facilities.
3.2.3
Organ Harvest and Preparation
At the animal facility, the mice were euthanized and organs harvested by authorized
personnel, this was done at diﬀerent time points post immunizations/infection (Figure
10, 11 and 12).
The organs, spleens from non-infected mice and lungs from infected
mice, were separately placed in tubes with cold RPMI (Table 2) and kept at 4◦C. For
homogenization were the spleens forced through a 100µm nylon cell strainer (BD Falcon),
followed by two washes with RMPI. The cells were then resuspended in RPMI with
Cell Supplement, HEPES, and 10% FCS. RMPI contains no proteins, lipids, or growth
factors and supplementation are needed for supporting cell viability and growth.
To
avoid clothing in the pull-down columns, the cells were forced through a 100µm nylon
cell strainer a second time. As lungs are more ﬁbrous than spleens, they were treated with
collagenase (Sigma-Aldrich) suspended in complete RPMI. Collagenase is an enzyme that
breaks peptide bonds in collagen, and thereby digest the lung tissue. The lungs were then
homogenized in GentleMACS (Miltenyl Biotec) C-tubes using a GentleMACS machine
(Miltenyl Biotec), and incubated in the collagenase solution for 1h at 37◦C. Where after
they received the same treatment as the spleens.
3.3
Tetramer staining and Enrichment
Isolated cells were stained at 37◦C for 30 minutes with antigen-speciﬁc MHC class II
tetramers and Fc block (Table 5), diluted in FACS buﬀer. The staining was followed by a
single wash with cold FACS buﬀer. Fc block inhibits non-speciﬁc binding of antibodies Fc
region to the Fc receptors on cells, assuring that observed antibody staining (Section 3.4)
is due to speciﬁc binding [63]. MHC class II tetramers have a complex structure (Figure
13), they consist of four MHC class II molecules each bound to a speciﬁc antigen. All four
molecules are biotinylated (labeled with biotin) that enables binding to streptavidin, a
tetrameric protein from Streptomyces avidinii, with four strongly biotin binding subunits
[64].
The streptavidin protein is labeled with the ﬂuorochrome Phycoerythrin (PE),
allowing the tetramer stained cells to be detected by ﬂow cytometry.
MHC class II
tetramers bind with high aﬃnity to the TCRs on CD4+ T cells speciﬁc for the bound
antigen, as the structure enables the binding of four TCR at the same time. Compared
to monomeric MHCs, which have a high dissociation rate, tetramers are more stable and
have higher binding activity [65].
==PAGE:44==
Experimental Procedures
25
Figure 13: MHC class II tetramer. Four biotinylated MHC Class II molecules (biotin in dark blue)
bound to a ﬂuorescent tetrameric streptavidin molecule by interaction with biotin. Each MHC Class II
molecules are bound to a speciﬁc antigenic peptide (With corrections [66])
Antigen speciﬁc CD4+ T cell are therefore strongly labeled, enabling puriﬁcation and fur-
ther investigations of their speciﬁcity and phenotype. Two diﬀerent tetramers were used:
MHC II ESAT-64−17 [67] and MHC II TB10.473−88 [68], used for cells from H56/CAF01
and BCG vaccinated mice respectively (Table 4). The diﬀerence in tetramers were due to
the lack of ESAT-6 in the BCG genome [69] and that H56 does not contain TB10.4. The
two proteins are both secreted by the Type VII secretion system and share immunolog-
ical characteristics, such as genome organization, size, antigenic properties and vaccine
potential. Though they diﬀer in their functions in M.tb growth and virulence, they are
both express continuously and strongly recognized during a M.tb infection[70]. Which
makes them suitable for comparing the T cell response after immunizations.

supplier.

Experimental Procedures
3.3.1
Enrichment
After tetramer staining, a magnetic bead-based pull-down procedure was performed to
enrich antigen-speciﬁc T cells.
The amount of antigen-speciﬁc T cells in the spleens
are low as the mice have only been immunized and not challenged. An enrichment can
thereby make FACS more eﬃcient, as the concentration of antigen-speciﬁc T cells will be
higher in the sample used for sorting [65]. The tetramer stained cells were resuspended
in cold FACS buﬀer with Anti-PE magnetic Microbeads (MACS Microbeads, Miltenyl
Biotech) and incubated at 4◦C for 30 minutes, followed by a wash with cold FACS buﬀer.
The Anti-PE magnetic microbeads attach to the PE on the tetramers, resulting in mag-
netic labeling, enabling the tetramer bound cells to be captured in magnetic columns.
The cells were resuspended in cold FACS buﬀer and passed over pre-rinsed MACS LS
columns (Miltenyl Biotech) placed on a MidiMACS magnetic ﬁeld (QuadroMACS, Mil-
tenyl Biotech). The LS columns consist of cell-friendly coated ferromagnetic spheres,
that when placed in a magnetic ﬁeld (MidiMACS) amplify their magnetic ﬁeld creating a
high gradient within the column [71]. The magnetically labeled cells will be held back in
the column, while non-labeled cells will run through. For a better yield, the samples were
applied to the columns twice. The columns were then washed three times with cold FACS
buﬀer, and then removed from the magnetic ﬁeld and placed in collection tubes. When
the columns are removed from the magnetic ﬁeld, the magnetic gradient will disappear
and held back cells can be eluded by pushing cold FACS buﬀer through the columns with
a plunger.
M.tb infected cells cannot be FACS sorted outside a BSL-III facility, and no sorter is
present at the SSI BSL-III facility. To improve the purity of tetramer speciﬁc cells from
M.tb infected lungs without a FACS, they were run over a second MACS LS Column
before RNA puriﬁcation (Section 3.6.1).
3.4
Surface Stain
To ensure a restricted gating for cell sorting and to determine expression of known sur-
face markers among the antigen-speciﬁc T cells, the cells were stained with a cocktail
of ﬂuorochrome-labeled antibodies diluted in BV buﬀer, at 4◦C for 30 minutes. After
incubation, the cells were washed twice in cold FACS buﬀer and put on ice. The cocktail
consisted of viability stain, anti-CD4-BV510, anti-CD44-FITC, and known lung-homing
important markers; anti-PD-1-BV605, anti-KLRG1-BV711, anti-CX3CR1-BV785, and
anti-CXCR3-BV421 (Table 5). The viability stain makes it possible to gate for live/dead
cells. The dye binds to cellular proteins, which there are increased access to in dead
cells, as they are more permeable. Dead cells will, therefore, have a more intensive stain.
With the anti-CD4 and anti-CD44, there can be gate for CD4+ T cells that have been
presented to antigen as CD44 is an activation marker.
