3.5
Cell Sorting
Cell sorting was performed on a FACS Aria-II at the Core facility for Flow Cytometry,
Copenhagen University. Control samples were used to set the gates, and the system was
run through with Ribonuclease (RNase) free water before sorting was initiated, to avoid
any RNase contamination of sorted samples. Live CD4+CD44+Tetramer+ T cells was
set as sorting gate, the gating strategy is seen in Figure 14. The cells were sorted directly
into lysis buﬀer containing a reducing agent to lyse and homogenize cells for subsequent
RNA puriﬁcation (Section 3.6).
Figure 14: Gating strategy for sorting of CD4+CD44+Tetramer+ T cells. First gate is for lymphocytes
using SSC and FSC, of these live CD4+ T cells, a CD4+ and viable stain gate are used and then
CD4+CD44+Tetramer+ T cells gate to be able to sort these cells
==PAGE:47==
28
Experimental Procedures
Fluorescence-activated cell sorting (FACS), use ﬂow cytometry to sort cells based on their
speciﬁc light scattering and ﬂuorescent characteristics. The cell samples enter a cuvette
when applied to the machine; here they will be surrounded by pressurized sheath ﬂuid
which hydrodynamically focus the sample ﬂuid into a narrow, rapidly ﬂowing stream.
The diameter of this stream can be changed by changing the pressure between the sheath
ﬂuid and the sample ﬂuid, a low-pressure diﬀerence create a narrow sample stream, while
higher pressure diﬀerence creates a broader sample stream (Figure 15).
The sample
stream should have a diameter that result in a single-cell stream, but as the ﬂow rate
decreases with a decreased diameter, a small diameter increases the time it takes to run a
sample. For the samples sorted in this project, the ﬂow rate was set relatively low so that
the rate was approximately 3000 events/second, which created a more precise sorting,
although time consuming [72].
Figure 15: Flow rate in FACS. The ﬂow rate can be change by changing the diﬀerence in pressure
around the sample ﬂuids and thereby its diameter, low diﬀerence create a narrow stream and a low ﬂow
rate, while high diﬀerence create a wide stream with high ﬂow rate.
Once the sample stream is focused in the cuvette it is intercepted by laser beams, Aria
II contains a blue (488nm), a red (633nm), and a violet (405nm) laser. When a cell
passes through the laser, it will cause the laser light to scatter.
The light scattered
axial to the laser beams is called forward scatter (FSC) and can indicate the size of the
cell, whereas the light scattered to the sides are called side scatter (SSC), which can
indicate the internal compositions of the cell [72]. When the laser hits the ﬂuorochrome-
labeled antibodies on the cells they will absorb photons (energy) and go into to an excited
electronic state. To return to the more stable ground state they must release the extra
energy, most of which will be emitted as light, known as ﬂuorescence. Some energy is lost
during this transitions, and the wavelength of ﬂuorescence is therefore always higher than
the excitation wavelength [73]. Diﬀerent ﬂuorochromes have diﬀerent emission maxima
and can thereby be distinguished (Appendix C). The scattered and ﬂuorescent light are
transferred to detector arrays by lenses and collection ﬁbers, the detector arrays will
convert light signals into electrical signals that can be displayed in a plot.
==PAGE:48==
Experimental Procedures
29
Sorting criteria is applied using the software (BD FACSDivaT M) in the form of gating
(Figure 14), if these criteria are met, an electrical charge will be applied to the stream.
Positioned below the lasers is a vibrating mechanism that causes the sample stream to
break into droplets. The droplets will in most cases contain one cell and if the sorting
criteria are meet be electrical charged. The droplet then passes through two strongly
charged deﬂection plates. Electrostatic attraction and repulsion will make the charges
droplets turn and fall into a collection tube, uncharged droplets are not aﬀected by the
electric ﬁeld and will, therefore, pass down the center to waste collector (Figure 16).
Figure 16: Cell sorting. After passing through the lasers, the stream of cells is broken into droplets
ideally containing a single cell. If the cells fulﬁll the sorting criteria, they will be electrically charged and
attracted to charged deﬂection plates, guiding them to fall into collection tubes. Non charged droplets,
containing cells that did not fulﬁll the sorting criteria will fall into a waste collector.
3.5.1
Flow-cytometric Analysis
Some samples were not sorted, such as control groups, samples for tetramer test (Section
4.0.1), responses for these samples were analyzed using an LSRFortessa ﬂow cytometer
(BD). A ﬂow cytometer is similar to a FACS, instead of sorting it only detects the
ﬂuorescent properties and display them in the plot were after all sample are directed to
waste collector. Infected samples were not sorted as it was not possible in a biosafety level
3 facility. They were instead put through pull-down twice to ensure further increment,
which was tested with ﬂow analysis after the cells were ﬁxed.
==PAGE:49==
30
Experimental Procedures
3.6
RNA Puriﬁcation
RNA puriﬁcation was performed with NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit (Machery-Nagel). Orig-
inally RNeasy MicroKit (Qiagen) was used, but as issues arose, the kit was tested against
the NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit, to which it was found inferior and therefore replaced (for
details see Section 4.0.2).
The cells were disrupted with the lysis buﬀer (RA1 buﬀer, Machery-Nagel) containing
tris(2-carboxyethyl)phosphine (TCEP (Machery-Nagel)) as a reducing agent. RA1 buﬀer
contains chaotropic salts that will disrupt all hydrophilic bonds leading to the lysis of
cell membranes and release of RNA. With the lysis of the cell membrane, RNases will
inevitably be released. The reducing agent can denture the RNases by reducing disulﬁde
bonds and thereby change the conformation of the enzyme leading to loss of function,
whereby degradation of RNA can be avoided.
The lysate was then homogenized by
vigorously pipetting. The homogenization step is important and serves to reduce the
viscosity of the cell lysates to prevent columns from clogging, which would lead to a
lower yield. After homogenization, 70% ethanol was added to the lysate before it was
applied to NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS Columns, ﬁtted with RNA binding silica membranes.
The high salt concentration of the RA1 buﬀer and addition of ethanol improves this
binding. Subsequent washes with MDB buﬀer (Machery-Nagel) desalts the membrane,
to make subsequent DNA digestion more eﬀective. Contaminating DNA can also bind to
the silica membrane, and should be removed. DNA digestion was performed on-column
with rDNase (Machery-Nagel) in a Reaction Buﬀer (Machery-Nagel). DNA should be
broken-down after incubation for 15 minutes, the rDNase is then inactivated with a wash
using RA2 buﬀer (Machery-Nagel). With the RNA bound to the columns, and contam-
inant DNA digested, all contaminants was washed away with two washes in buﬀer RA3
(Machery-Nagel); the second wash had a prolonged spin in order to dry the column, mak-
ing sure that no ethanol is carried over during the subsequent RNA elution, as ethanol
can interfere with downstream reactions. To ﬁnally elute total RNA from the columns,
RNase-free water was added and columns centrifuged.
3.6.1
RNA puriﬁcation of infected tissue
As RA1 buﬀer does not kill all M.tb bacteria, a TRIzol based RNA puriﬁcation kit;
Direct-zol RNA Microprep (Zymo Research), was used for the infected cells. Cells were
ﬁrst lysed with TRIzol, which disrupt all cells and kill the bacteria. Ethanol is then added,
were after the lysate was applied directly to Zymo-spin IC columns (Zymo Research),
which have similar work ﬂow as NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS columns, but work with TRIzol
based lysates. On column DNase treatment was likewise performed and columns were
washed three times before puriﬁed RNA was eluded in 8µl RNase Free water.
==PAGE:50==
Experimental Procedures
31
3.6.2
Quality Measurements
For further applications, it is important to know the concentration, purity, and integrity
of RNA. Quality measurements was mostly done with an 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent),
some samples was analyzed on a Nanodrop (Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc). The purity of
RNA can be determined by the absence of contaminants and gDNA. Amount of contami-
nants and the RNA concentration can be measured by the absorbance of UV light. RNA
absorbs UV light at a wavelength of 260nm. In a Nanodrop a sample can be exposed to
UV light, and the absorbance can be measured. The amount of light that is absorbed
with the wavelength of 260nm determines the RNA concentration. The ratio readings
at 260nm and 280nm (A260/A280) and at 260nm and 230nm (A260/A230) provides an
estimate of the purity of RNA concerning contaminants that absorb in UV spectra. The
260/280 ratio is used to assess contamination with protein and should be around 1.8-2.1.
The 260/230 ration should be 2.0-2.2, low ratio are often associated with organic com-
pounds and salts, can be salts left from puriﬁcation.
The integrity of RNA is determined by the degree of degradation; no degradation corre-
lates with high integrity. Low degree of degradation is essential for downstream analysis.
A Nanodrop cannot determine degradation and integrity analysis was done with a Bio-
analyzer, which also provides RNA concentration. Samples and an RNA ladder were
loaded onto a chip that was inserted in the Bioanalyzer. The Bioanalyzer use automated
electrophoresis, to separate RNA samples, which can be detected via laser-induced ﬂu-
orescence detection.
It generates a gel-like image and electropherogram that provide
sample concentration and integrity. Ribosomal RNA makes up more than 80 % of the
total RNA, can can therefore be used for integrity measurements. RNA from eukary-
otic cells should display clearly deﬁned 28S and a 18S (ribosomal subunits) bands. The
amount of 28S should be double the intensity of the 18S, and the ratio of 28S/18S is
ideally close to 2. If the ribosomal bands or peaks of a speciﬁc sample are not sharp
and appear as a smear, there are properly high amounts of degradation in the sample.
The Bioanalyzer also provide RNA integrity number (RIN), which indicates integrity as
a value between 1 and 10. RIN of 1 is giving to samples that are highly degraded and
RIN of 10 if the RNA is mostly intact, values between 7 and 10 are indicators of RNA
quality suitable for downstream applications. RIN is an algorithm that includes several
diﬀerent electropherograms parameters including the 28S/18S ratio.
3.7
Nanostring Gene Expression Assay
Nanostring gene expression assay (Nanostring Technologies) is a digital counting tech-
nology that enables counting of up to 800 target genes per samples. Individual target
mRNA are thus labeled with uniquely ﬂuorescent barcodes, belonging to a speciﬁc Code-
Set, enabling them to be counted in a nCounter instrument.
==PAGE:51==
32
Experimental Procedures
The CodeSet consists of two probes. A Reporter probe with a unique color-coded ﬂuo-
rescent barcode on its 5’ and a Capture probe which carries a biotin moiety on its 3’ end.
Both probes have a target-speciﬁc sequence and can thereby hybridize to their speciﬁc
target gene to form a Target-Probe complex (Figure 17). The CodeSet used was nCounter
Mouse Immunology Panel (NanoString Technologies), containing 547 immunology-related
mouse genes and 14 controls for quality control.
Figure 17:
nCounter CodeSet Chemestry.
The Target-Probe complex of a nCounter CodeSet
(right) consist of a Capture probe and Reporter probe attached to a speciﬁc target gene (left). The
Capture probe carries a biotin moiety on its 3’ end and the Reporter probe carries a unique color-coded
ﬂuorescent barcode on its 5’
3.7.1
RNA ampliﬁcation and cDNA conversion
Puriﬁed RNA can be used in Nanostring assays, with a recommended sample input for
nCounter XT assays on 100 ng RNA [74]. As seen in Tables 9, 10, and 11 RNA con-
centrations from the isolated samples in this project were low, and pre-ampliﬁcation
was therefore necessary. It was done using nCounter Low RNA Input Ampliﬁcation kit
(NanoString Technologies), which includes two steps; conversion of RNA into cDNA,
and multi-target enrichment (MTE) amplifying the cDNA using target-speciﬁc primers
corresponding to the CodeSet. cDNA conversion was done by addition of reverse tran-
scriptases (RTs) and primers for reverse transcription. Samples were placed in a thermal
cycler for a three-step cDNA conversion process; 1) Incubation at 25◦C for 10 minutes,
allowing primers to anneal to mRNA, 2) Incubation at 42◦C for 60 minutes, for cDNA
synthesis by RTs, and 3) Incubation at 85◦C for 5 minutes, to inactivate RTs. cDNA
was then ampliﬁed by The Multi-Target Enrichment (MTE). Ampliﬁcation polymerase
and buﬀer mixed with a pool of gene-speciﬁc primers corresponding to the CodeSet were
added to samples containing cDNA. Ampliﬁcation was performed in a thermal cycler
with a denaturation step at 95◦C for 10 minutes, followed by 10 cycles of a two-step
ampliﬁcation: Denaturation at 95◦C for 15 seconds and annealing at 60◦C for 4 minutes.
After the 10 cycles, the samples were incubated for 2 minutes at 95◦C and quickly put
on the ice, where after they were stores at -80◦C [75].
==PAGE:52==
Experimental Procedures
33
3.7.2
Hybridization and Reading
The samples were hybridized and read by BioXpedia, Århus, Denmark. Here probes
from CodeSet was added to samples in excess to be sure all targets were matched with
probes. Hybridization was performed at 65◦C for at least 16 hours. After hybridization,
the samples were loaded onto a cartridge (nCounter SPRINT Cartridge for 12 assays
(NanoString Technologies)), at the loading port and the cartridge was placed into an
nCounter SPRINT Proﬁler.
In the instrument excess probes and non-target cellular
transcripts were washed away with a multichannel microﬂuidics system. The cartridge
contains a streptavidin-coated slide to which the target probe complex can bind to, by the
biotin. By electro-current, the complexes were then immobilized and aligned horizontally.
The instrument then scanned the cartridge through an automated ﬂuorescent microscope,
enabling counting of each reporter barcode (Figure 18).
The counts for each target
molecule was tabulated in a comma-separated value (CSV) format for analysis in the
free nSolver analysis software (NanoString Technologies) [76]. Most of the analysis and
statistical measurements was made in R.
Figure 18: nCounter Assay Overview. Excess probes are washed away in hybridized samples. The
probe/target complexes bound to the cartridge are immobilized and align horizontally so the color codes
(barcodes) can be counted. Counts are tabulated for each target molecule (With modiﬁcations [77].)
==PAGE:53==
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4
Process Optimization
Obtaining RNA in suﬃcient quantity and quality for downstream applications from MHC
II Tetramer sorted memory CD4+ T cells is setting the bar high. This is due to the low
number of memory cells present and their low transcriptomal activity. The process is
complicated and includes several challenging procedures. Although all individual steps
were established individually, issues occurred when they were performed in continuum
(Section 19). The ﬁrst issue that arose was a low number cell, for sorting, after tetramer
pull-down. Which, was thought to be the primary underlying reason for the following
failed RNA puriﬁcation.
The issue was solved by optimizing the tetramer pull-down
procedure (Section 4.0.1). It subsequently turned out that even with higher cell numbers,
the RNA puriﬁcation still failed.
Optimization was therefore also performed on the
procedure for RNA puriﬁcation (Section 4.0.2), before it was possible to obtain enough
RNA with suﬃcient quality to proceed with the Nanostring gene expression assay.
Figure 19: Process Optimization Overview. The ﬁrst attempts at running a full procedure exper-
iment (tetramer pull-down →FACS →RNA puriﬁcation) were unsuccessful (gray) as issues occurred
(Light Green). The issues were solved by optimization’s (Dark Green), and full procedure experiments
could be performed (Black).
4.0.1
Tetramer Pull-down Optimization
The ﬁrst tetramer pull-down experiments indicated that the reason for the low amount
of cells could be the conditions of the tetramers. NIH claims that their MHC Class II
tetramers are stable in 3-6 months, when stored at 4◦C, however stability varies between
MHC Class II tetramers with diﬀerent speciﬁcity [78]. Experiments at SSI with NIH
MHC Class II tetramers have previously shown that AG85B-speciﬁc tetramers have a
relatively short shelf life of 3-4 months, whereas ESAT-6-speciﬁc tetramers have shown
to be stable beyond 10 months. Tetramers speciﬁc for TB10.4 appears to have a shelf
life of at least 8 months. However, it has also been the experience that the tetramer
performance decrease over time and that stability are fairly batch dependent.
==PAGE:55==
36
Process Optimization
The tetramers used in the ﬁrst experiments were from 14-10-2016; MHC class II ESAT-6-
speciﬁc tetramer (Batch:31938) and MHC class II TB10.4-speciﬁc tetramer (batch:31941)
(Table 4), at the time of use batches were of age 10-12 months. Figure 20 shows the fre-
quency of tetramer+CD44+ T cells, in spleens 5 weeks post immunization, after tetramer
pull-down. A MHC II ESAT-6 tetramer+ population is seen in live CD4+ T cells from
H56/CAF01 immunized mice, though within the lower range of what has been seen be-
fore in comparable time points post immunization [43]. A MHC II TB10.4 tetramer+
population was not detected in live CD4+ T cells from mice immunized with BCG. The
lack of MHC II TB10.4 speciﬁc tetramer+ T cells in BCG mice compared to MHC II
ESAT-6 tetramer+ T cells in H56/CAF01 mice could be due to an instability of the MHC
II TB10.4 tetramer. Suboptimal performance of the MHC II ESAT-6 tetramer+ could
also be likely. New tetramers of both peptides were therefore ordered, to ensure optimal
condition for the tetramer pull-down experiments.
Figure 20: Insuﬃcient Tetramer Enrichment - 2016 tetramers. Tetramer pull-down with 2016
tetramers, week 5 post immunization. MHC class II tetramer+CD44+ cells in spleen from mice immu-
nizedwith H56/CAF01 (MHC II ESAT-6 speciﬁc tetramer) and in mice immunized with BCG (MHC II
TB10.4 speciﬁc tetramer) after tetramer pull-down. Flow-Jo and GraphPad Prism.
In the meantime a full procedure experiment was performed, though only with mice
immunized with H56/CAF01 and thereby just staining with MHC II ESAT-6 speciﬁc
tetramers (2016) that had been shown to work. On ﬁgure 21 it can be seen that the
vaccine is capable of priming ESAT-6 speciﬁc CD4+ memory T cells. There is a clear
population of tetramer+CD44+ T cells in the mice immunized 12 weeks prior to sampling,
compared to the saline group. It was only possible to sort ∼1700 MHC class II ESAT-
6 tetramer+CD44+ T cells/spleen from the immunized group.
On the basis of other
experiments, the number of MHC class II ESAT-6 tetramer+CD44+ T cells was expected
to be higher ∼3000-5000 cells/spleen [43]. RNA puriﬁcation was done with RNeasy Micro
kit, yet analyses with Bioanalyzer could not detect RNA in any samples (Appendix D.1.4).
Unsuccessful RNA puriﬁcation could be due to the low number of sorted cells, possibly
combined with unoptimized RNA Puriﬁcation procedure. Leading back to the conclusion
that probably there was a performance issue for the 2016 tetramers.

D.1.5). The pull-down performance check showed remaining tetramer positive T cells in
the ﬂow-through after tetramer pull-down (Figure 22).

==PAGE:57==
38
Process Optimization
This result indicates that the MHC II ESAT-6 tetramers from this batch was capable of
binding to their cognitive TCR, but were not suﬃciently held back in the columns. This
could either be due to issues with the binding of Anti-PE beads to the tetramer+ cells
allowing them to be retained within the magnetic LS columns, or with the LS columns
(e.g., trapped air, inadequate pre-vetting, incorrect positioning).
On the basis of experiments performed, an optimization strategy was developed. Besides
the use of new tetramers, for optimal staining performance, the strategy included addi-
tional steps. To ensure proper staining, the cells were stained in a 200µl Fc block/Tetramer
volume (previously 100µl). The ﬂow-through after tetramer pull-down was reapplied to
the magnetic LS columns in order to maximize tetramer+ cell yield prior to elution of
positive fraction. Anti-PE beads further tend to clog/aggregate so before use proper
vortexing (min 1 minute) should be enforced. As tetramers are mostly stable at 4◦C
all reagents should be held cold throughout experimentation. Once the new tetramers
arrived (Table 4), a full experimental procedure including the optimization steps men-
tioned above was performed. It was now possible to sort ∼5000 Tetramer+CD44+ T
cells/spleen from BCG immunized mice using the new MHC class II TB10.4 tetramer
(37091: 2017-10-23). From H56/CAF01 immunized mice it was possible to sort 4511
Tetramer+CD44+ T cells using MHC class II ESAT-6 tetramer (37088: 2017-10-23)
from one mice, but only 1370 cells from the other (only two samples were left in this
group).
In both groups a clear tetramer+CD44+ T cell population was visible, with
ESAT-6 Tetramer+ cells having higher Median Fluorescence Intensity (MFI) compared
to staining with the 2016 tetramers, indicating improved staining (Figure 23).
Figure 23: Tetramer+CD44+ T cells - 2017 tetramers. First full procedure with 2017 tetramers.
MHC class II tetramer+CD44+ speciﬁc T cells in spleens from a mice immunized with H56/CAF01 and
BCG after tetramer pull-down. 17 weeks post immunization. Graph: MFI of 2017 PE-MHC II ESAT-6
tetramer compared with 2016 PE-MHC II ESAT-6 tetramer. Flow-Jo and GraphPad Prism.

4.0.2
RNA puriﬁcation Optimization
The enrichment procedure before sorting was optimized to gain the highest cell yield
possible by sorting. However, RNA puriﬁcation was still not successful despite the higher
amount of cells sorted. Hence optimization of the RNA puriﬁcation process. Initially,
the performance of two RNeasy kits at diﬀerent cell numbers was investigated, in a
titration experiment, using naive splenocytes as input cells. RNA were puriﬁed using
either RNeasy Minikit for samples with high cell numbers (5x106, 1x106, and 2x105) and
RNeasy Micro kit for samples with low cell numbers (4x104, 8x103, and 1.6x103). Table
6 shows that the RNeasy Microkit can purify RNA down to 1600 cells. For both kits,
low cell numbers correlated with a decrease in yield and concentration. RNeasy Mirokit
was chosen to be used in further experiments as it performed better at low cell numbers
and the cell number from cell sorting in this project never reached 4X104 cells.

When purifying RNA, it is essential to avoid the presence of Ribonucleases (RNase),
enzymes that catalyze the degradation of RNA. The RNA procedure, therefore, included
prevention of RNase contamination using RNase free tubes, tips, and water as well as
using gloves and long sleeved lab coat, and cleaning the workplace with a decontamina-
tion reagent, such as RNase AWAY. After the unsuccessful RNA puriﬁcations previously
described, extra awareness for avoiding contamination was added to the workﬂow. Cells
also contain RNase themselves, which can be released following cell death or cell lysis,
and it is therefore important to prevent RNA degradation when cells are lysed. Cell
sorting is stressful to the cells due to high pressure exceted, and they can therefore lyse
during the sort process, leading to release of RNases into the collection tube. A lysis
buﬀer such as RLT will disrupt hydrophilic bonds in the cells, and lyse the cell mem-
brane. This will lead to release of RNA as well as RNases, but as the RLT buﬀer contains
a high concentration of chaotropic salts it will inactivate the RNases [79]. To prevent
RNA degradation the cells were sorted directly into RLT buﬀer rather than PBS with
FCS. When sorted into a buﬀer with high salt concentration the cells can ﬂoat on the
surface of the buﬀer, it is therefore important to invert and mix the tube right after cell
sorting.
Despite the optimization of the tetramer staining and pull-down enrichment procedure
as well as extra awareness of RNA degradation during the RNA puriﬁcation procedure, it
was still diﬃcult to obtain good quality RNA from the sorted cells. Figure 25 shows the
Gel-like image from the RNA puriﬁcation after cell sorting and tetramer enrichment with
2017 tetramers. No clear RNA bands was seen, so no RNA is detected, and the smear
indicated RNA degradation. Further initiatives are needed to avoid RNA degradation,
and it could be an indication that the RNase Micro kit might not be suﬃcient enough.

In an attempt to obtain a better yield, another RNA puriﬁcation kit was tried out: the
NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit. The procedure for this kit is very similar to the RNeasy Mirco
kit, with only a few diﬀerences. NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS columns have a smaller diameter,
which can make the puriﬁcation more eﬃcient and allow for a smaller elution volume. It
also contains its own buﬀers, although with the same main functions as the once found in
RNeasy Micro kit. However, the cells are lysed in a larger volume, which might facilitate
lysis and RNase inhibition. The NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit procedure is more simple and
avoids too many spins with open lids, which can be diﬃcult to performed and might
lead to contamination. Table 7 and Figure 26 show the performance of both kits using
diﬀerent cell input numbers. The NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS Kit appears to have a better
performance at low cell input numbers regarding RNA integrity. Hence the RNeasy Micro
kit was replaced for NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit for remaining experiments.

A reason for the degraded RNA in puriﬁed samples could be due to inadequate inhibition
of RNases. To ensure eﬃcient inhibition, a reducing agent such as β-ME or TCEP, was
added to the lysis buﬀers, as reducing agents can irreversibly denature RNAses. TCEP
was chosen as it is odor free compared to β-ME. The combined use of NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA
XS kit and the addition of a reducing agent to the lysis buﬀer optimized the RNA puriﬁ-
cation procedure. In combination with the other optimizations carried out, it was ﬁnally
feasible to obtain RNA in suﬃcient quantity and quality to be used for the Nanostring
gene expression assay. Table 8 and Figure 27 show the quality measurements from a
full procedure experiment, were 3 mice were pooled within the groups to secure high cell
numbers.

4.0.3
Conclusion of Optimizations
After immunization, there will be a low amount of memory cells, leading to small amounts
of RNA. Trying to obtain RNA in suﬃcient quantity and quality from tetramer sorted
memory CD4+ T cells for downstream applications has showed to be diﬃcult. However,
the diﬀerent process optimizations described in this chapter made it feasible. Optimal
conditions for tetramers are crucial for an eﬃcient tetramer staining and enrichment.
The performance of tetramers decreases with time, as they can become unstable, though
batch and speciﬁcity depended. Optimal staining performance requires the use of new
tetramers, increased staining volume, and ensuring cold condition throughout the ex-
periment. Flowthrough after tetramer pull-down has to be reapplied to the columns to
maximize yield. There are several critical factors for RNA puriﬁcation. Eﬃcient cell lysis
can be obtained by directly sort cell into a lysis buﬀer containing a reducing agent to
inhibit the released RNases. Higher yield and a lower elute volume cen be secured with
the use of NucleoSpin R
⃝RNA XS kit with a more straightforward procedure and smaller
diameter of its columns than the RNeasy Micro kit. The optimization made it possible to
obtain samples (both pooled and individual) with RNA in suﬃcient quantity and quality
for the Nanostring gene expression assay (Section 5.1).

Twelve samples of puriﬁed RNA was obtained, consisting of a group of memory T cells
and a group eﬀector T cells. The memory group consisted of sorted MHC II tetramers+
CD4+ T cells from the spleens of immunized mice (F2427) and includes three H56/CAF01
samples (Table 9) and three BCG samples (Table 10). The eﬀector group consisted of
MHC II tetramers+ CD4 T cell, enriched by double pull-down, from lungs from mice
that were immunized and then infected with M.tb (F2340), this group includes two saline
samples, two H56/CAF01 samples, and two BCG samples (Table 11). Electropherograms
and Gel-Like images for each sample can be found in Appendix D.

The RIN factor in all samples was close to 10 and most of the sample have 28S/18S ratio
close to 2, which indicate a high quality of the puriﬁed RNA, sample 7 and 12 show lower
quality than the other samples. Sample 1, 2, and 4 each consisted of a pool of three
individual samples within their immunization groups. Sample 1 and 4 are from the full
procedure experiment with pooled groups, mentioned in the previous section (Table 8
and Figure 27). Sample 2 was from a later full procedure experiment, where issues arose
in obtaining enough MHC II ESAT-6 tetramers+ CD4 T cells from individual samples.
When samples from individual mice are pooled, the biological variation is lost as it is im-
possible to determine any possible outliers. That should be taken into consideration when
assessing the results and comparing the samples. Another consideration was the diﬀer-
ence in weeks after immunization, which is not consistent in the memory group, and the
gene expression is therefore not 100% comparable. However, both immunization groups
consist of a memory response around week 12, which make them capable of giving an
insight into a resting memory response. The eﬀector group only contains two samples of
each immunization strategy, three samples would have gained a higher biological variance.
The samples in the eﬀector group had a higher concentration of RNA, which was re-
lating to a much higher immunological activity and antigen stimulation during infection.
Resting memory cells after immunization will have a lower activity level and thus be
expected to have a lower RNA concentration. Before cDNA conversion and ampliﬁcation
the samples were corrected by dilution, so they all consisted of 4µl with a speciﬁc mass of
RNA. Due to the diﬀerence in RNA concentrations, the memory group contained a mass
of 0.7ng and eﬀector group a mass of 2ng. Sample 8 only contained RNase free-water
due to an error in the laboratory during cDNA conversion.
==PAGE:66==
Results
47
5.2
Gene Expression Data
Gene expression analysis compare the expression levels of multiple genes between the
samples. The data from Nanostring assay was a raw data ﬁle containing quantiﬁcation of
messenger RNA (mRNA) levels of speciﬁc genes, in the form of barcode counts. Figure
28 shows the work-ﬂow that was performed for the gene expression analysis.
Before
analysis the data were run through a Quality Control (QC) to evaluate the performance
of the assay, then a background correction and normalization was performed to remove
nonbiological variation, enabling biological comparison between samples.
Figure 28: Work-ﬂow of Gene Expression Analysis The raw data were run through a Quality
Control (QC), and Normalization before analysis (Dark Green). These steps included several sub steps
(Light Green).
The raw data shows that the count for most of the endogenous genes was relatively
low. The QC was passed, so the problem did not lie in the performance of the assay
or hybridization.
However, the binding Density indicated that the amount of input
was low (Section 5.2.1). The reason for the low count could, therefore, be insuﬃcient
ampliﬁcation. A correct concentration estimate was essential for choosing the number of
ampliﬁcation cycles. However, quantifying a low amount of RNA can be diﬃcult. The
measured RNA concentration on the Bioanalyzer may have been over- or under-estimated,
leading to a wrong estimation of ampliﬁcation cycles to run. In this project, the ten
cycles that were run seems not to have been enough. Optimization should have been
performed to ﬁnd the right amount of cycles, which could ensure a suﬃcient ampliﬁcation
(See Discussion).
After the background was subtracted counts for some target genes
was detected, indicating that cDNA synthesis was successful and that some level of
ampliﬁcation did occur. The targets with counts above background may be genes that
are highly expressed and therefore in spite of the low input still give detectable counts.
The gene expression proﬁle in this experiment might therefore not be the whole proﬁle,
but only the highly expressed targets (Figure 29). It cannot be determined what level of
expression the targets that are below the background would have. This should be taken
into consideration when examining the results of the experiment.
==PAGE:67==
48

Due to the low input, the result that are detected may only be information from highly expressed genes
(Dark green). Information about genes with low or no expression are lost (Light Green).

Table 12, most QC measurements passed as they fulﬁll the acceptance criteria. It can,
therefore, be concluded that the technical performance or the hybridization was not the
source of error for the low gene count.

49
Binding Density
The binding density is the number of ﬂuorescent spots per square micron that are bound
to the scare surface. A high density could mean overlapping count codes and imaging
saturation, leading to loss of data. The nCounter calculated the number of ﬂuorescent
spots per square micron that were bound to the surface, for each sample [74]. The number
should be between 0.1-1.8; the binding densities in this experiment is therefore rather
low. The low binding density indicates that the starting material was similarly low, as the
relationship between input amount and binding density is linear. This correlated to the
low counts that were observed in the raw data. The low binding density resulted in six
ﬂagged samples. However, the binding densities were close to the acceptance criteria. The
most prominent concern for binding density it too high densities, exceeding saturation,
so the binding density QC was passed for all samples.
Imaging QC
Imaging QC assesses the performance of the imaging process. The cartridge contains a
lane for each sample, the total surface of these lanes is divided into individual Fields of
view (FOV) (Figure 30), which are scanned one at a time to count barcodes. The total
counts for the entire sample are the sum of counts in all FOV. The performance of this
imaging was calculated as the ratio between the number of FOVs that have been scanned
with success (FOV Counted) and the number of FOV that was attempted to be scanned
(FOV Count), the performance should be above 75% and the ratio thereby above 0.75
(Equation 5.1). All sample perform close to 100% (Table 12), so nothing has aﬀected the
imaging performance.
Imaging QC = FOV Counted

Figure 30:
nCounter Cartidge.
A nCounter Cartridge, have a lane for each sample, the lanes
contains a streptavidin coded slide, to which the target-probe complexes bind to. The total surface area
is divided into small ﬁelds; Individual Field of view (FOV). When the instrument scans the cartridge,
the FOVs are scanned one at the time, and counts are summed over all FOVs [74].
==PAGE:69==
50
Results
QC with Control Samples
The CodeSet contained twelve control samples, six positive controls, and six negative
controls. The positive controls are synthetic DNA targets with a concentration range of
0.125, 0.5, 2.0, 8.0, and 128 femtomolar (fM). The concentration increase linearly and
the resulting counts should as well.
For each sample, this correlation was tested by,
plotting the concentrations of the positive controls versus their counts. The gradient
was the positive control linearity QC measurement, which should be above 0.95. In all
samples, the gradient was close to 1 indicating good assay performance in all lanes [74].
To validate the assay’s limit of detection, and thereby its sensitivity. The performance of
the positive controls was compared with the negative controls, which are probes with no
target. The average counts of the negative controls plus two times the standard deviation
(SD) should be higher than the lowest positive controls [74]. Figure 31 shows that the
positive controls with the lowest concentrations (0.125, 0.5 fM) had a higher count than
the negative control for all samples. The limit of detection was therefore low, giving the
assay a high sensitivity, which was a good asset with the low count dataset in this project.
Figure 31: Limit of Detection. The average counts of the negative controls plus two times standard
deviation (SD) are lower than the counts for the two positive controls with the lowest concentration.
The limit of detection is therefore validated. Graphpad Prism. POS = Positive Control, NEG = Negative
Control
5.2.2
Background
Background noise, and thereby false positives could be present in the assay, variating
between lanes. For high expression experiments, this background noise is insigniﬁcant as
the actual counts will precede the number of false positives. However, for assays with
low counts, as seen in this project, the background signals needs to be calculated and
limited as the false positives can alter the result [74]. The background was calculated from
the negative control groups as their average plus two times of SD, and then subtracted
from all samples. Counts that were below background were assigned a value of one for
the subsequent analysis. Genes which no counts above background in any samples were
eliminated from the dataset. This resulted in a signiﬁcant reduction of the dataset as only
140 of the 548 targets remained. Number of targets above background varied between
samples (Figure 32). However, there were clearly detected expression above background
for some targets, and there must have been some biological impact.
==PAGE:70==
Results
51
Figure 32:
Targets above Background.
Target genes above background in each sample.
Left:
Resting Memory MHC II Tetramer+ T cells from spleens 11-14 weeks after immunizations. right: Eﬀector
samples 5 weeks after infection and 71 weeks after immunization, from lung. Graphpad Prism
5.2.3
Normalization
Normalization of data was done to remove technical variability from the experiment. It
balanced the data between samples, making it possible to make meaningful biological
comparisons between them. Normalization multiplied each gene count with two diﬀerent
normalization factors. The Positive Control Normalization factor (PCN factor) adjust
the data for variations that exist across samples and lanes. Variations could have arisen
by variables in user technique, hybridization, puriﬁcation, complex to slide binding and
imaging [74]. The PCN factor was calculated from the six positive controls in the CodeSet
(Equation 5.2), and should be between 0.3-3. To normalize the data, the background
subtracted counts for each target were multiplied by the sample’s PCN factor (Table 13).
PCN factorsample1 =
Mean of Geometric meanssample1−12
Geometric mean of positive controlssample1
(5.2)
CodeSet Content factor (CSC factor) corrects for the variability in the sample input,
such as variance in the amount and quality of RNA. As the RNA was low in the input
samples and it was diﬃcult to measure precise low RNA concentration there was a large
possibility for variances. The CSC factor was calculated from the housekeeping genes
in the CodeSet (Equation 5.3), these are mRNA targets known to show no or little
variability in expression across diﬀerent conditions [74]. The variances in these genes
were, therefore, most likely due to amount and quality of RNA. As sample 8 does not
contain any target, this sample was excluded from the CSC calculation. To normalize
the data the background was subtracted and PCN normalized counts for each gene were
multiplied by the samples CSC factor which should be between 0.1-10.
CSC factorsample1 =
Mean of Geometric meanssample1−12
Geometric mean of Housekeeping genessample1
(5.3)

the CSC factor was, therefore, calculated for each group when comparing samples within
the groups and calculated across all samples when comparing the two groups against
each other. This created three data ﬁles. DataAll CSC normalized across all samples,
DataMemory, CSC normalized for sample 1-6, and DataEffector CSC normalized across
samples 7-12 minus sample 8. Note that the PCN normalization was done over all samples
as this factor correct for variability in technical performance (Normalized data sample
can be found in Appendix H). Figure 33 shows the raw counts of housekeeping genes

from each sample. In the memory group (sample 1-6) there was little variation between
the housekeeping counts in each sample, indicating low variation in input for this group.
More variation was seen within the eﬀector group. It was seen that sample 8 contained
no targets, as it had no expression. Sample 11 and sample 12 were outliers, sample 11
showed high expression, and there was almost no expression of any genes in sample 12.
The input mass in sample 11 may have been larger than expected, which could be due to
diﬃculties precise RNA measurements. The Bioanalyzer Pico kit used to determine RNA
concentrations can detect between 250-5000 pg/µl. In table 11, it can be seen that the
concentration of RNA in sample 12 was 4834 pg/µl, thus in the top range of the detection
range of this kit. When the upper limit is reached, the concentration measurements will
saturate and not give the right concentration. The concentration in sample 11 could thus
have been underestimated and in reality be higher, leading to a higher mass in the input
for the assay. The opposite might have happened in sample 12. Table 11 shows the RNA
concentration of sample 12 to be 551pg/µl, which is at the low end of the range. The
RNA concentration, therefore, might have been overestimated. The same phenomenon
was seen for sample 2 and 3, as also their RNA concentrations were close to the limit of
the kit. Sample 11 should have been diluted more and run again to have ensured correct
measurement of RNA concentration.

5.3
Gene Expression Analysis
A gene expression analysis can give an insight into the cellular process of the speciﬁc
T cells, leading to an understanding of the functions of the cell. The primary focus of
this thesis was to investigate the diﬀerence in expression of M.tb speciﬁc T cells from
H56/CAF01 and BCG immunized mice. It was done in a memory setting with sorted
antigen-speciﬁc resting memory CD4+ T cells from spleens 11-14 weeks after immuniza-
tion and an eﬀector setting with tetramer pull-down enriched antigen-speciﬁc secondary
eﬀector CD4+ T cells from lungs 71 weeks after immunization and 5 weeks after infec-
tion with M.tb. Diﬀerence between TB10.4+ speciﬁc CD4+ T cells from mice immunized
with BCG were compared with ESAT-6+ speciﬁc CD4+ T cells from mice immunized
with H56/CAF01. Several factors as mentioned above should be taken into consideration
when interpreting these results. Information about the genes highlighted in the analysis
can be found in Appendix F.
5.3.1
Memory & Eﬀector
Eﬀector and memory cells diﬀer in functions and properties, a diﬀerence in gene expres-
sion is therefore expected. However, a direct comparison between the memory group
and the eﬀector group in this thesis could not be made as the samples did not share
the same conditions such as; tissue or isolation method. Nevertheless, looking at the
diﬀerences between these two types of T cells gave an insight into possible expression
diﬀerence, with reservations concerning the sample diﬀerences. It also gave an insight
into the performance of the speciﬁc CD4+ T cell isolation methods and how that aﬀected
the result. Pearson correlation was made between samples concerning their counts for
each gene and visualized in a correlation heatmap (Figure 34.A). Sample 8 was be seen
as an outliner, as it has low correlation with the other samples. Which was expected as
the sample only contained RNase free-water, and it was therefore excluded from further
analysis. Samples within the same group (eﬀector or memory) were more correlated than
samples between the two groups.
==PAGE:73==
54
Results
There was a higher correlation between eﬀector samples (sample 7-12) than between mem-
ory samples (sample 1-6). Figure 34.B showed the correlation between memory samples,
and they all correlated similar to each other, with no signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the
two immunizations.
Figure 34.C show the correlation between eﬀector samples, with
sample 8 excluded. Most of the eﬀector samples were highly correlated to each other,
besides from sample 12 that was seen as an outliner. Overall, a diﬀerence between the
memory and eﬀector samples could be observed, but no clear diﬀerences between the two
immunizations within the two groups.

Performing statistics on the average counts in each sample showed that the memory
group is signiﬁcantly diﬀerent from the eﬀector group with a P-value of 0.0174.
To
determine if this diﬀerence was caused by biology or technical variances other methods
were used. Figure 35 shows a heatmap, visualizing the gene expression for each sample.
The twelve samples had three genes with high expression in common: Lcp2, B2m, and
Cd74.
The Lcp2 protein is involved in TCR signaling and thereby T cell activation.
However, Cd74 and B2m encoded proteins are involved in antigen presentation on MHC II
and I respectively, and the former thus more associated with macrophages and monocytes
than T cells.
==PAGE:74==
Results
55
The eﬀector samples expressed a large variety of genes and especially two samples, sample
9 and 11, had high expression of a large pool of genes. Expected as the input amount
probably was higher for these two samples (Section 5.2.3). Sample 12 was seen to have
low expression of most genes, consistent with the low correlations to other samples seen
in the correlation heatmap. The memory group samples expressed a lower number of
genes than the eﬀector group, again expected as the gene input for these samples was
lower (0.7ng vs. 2.0ng) - despite normalization, an eﬀect of this could not be excluded.
In the memory group was there a slight indication of a diﬀerence in gene expression of
CD4+ T cells primed by H56/CAF01 vs. BCG.

By PCA analysis the data can be compressed into a 2D plot that captures the essence
of the original data. The correlations (or lack thereof) between the samples for each
gene was used to make Principal Components (PC) that described the variation in the
data.
Each sample had a PC score for each PC, made from the count of each gene
and the inﬂuence on the PC. The scores were plotted, and correlated samples should
cluster together [80]. Figure 36 is the PCA plot with PC1 and PC2. The two PCs only
counted for 64.8%, and the impact factors for each gene were relatively low. However,
the memory samples grouped together and separated from the eﬀector samples, along
PC1. The eﬀector samples did not group in the same manner. Sample 11 and 9 which
have shown to express most genes separated away from the memory samples along PC1.
As sample 12 was an outlier, it was taken out of the analysis.
==PAGE:75==
56
Results
Genes that aﬀected the PCs are depicted in Figure 36.B and 36.C. From PC1 it was
seen that the memory population were formed by the low or no expression of genes that
were expressed by sample 9 and 11. These included Plaur, Icam1, Irf1, Tgfb1. Plaur and
Icam codes for proteins involved in migration, though mostly associated with other cell
types than T cells. Tgfb1 can promote Tregs and dampen immune functions, whereas Irf1
regulate IFN-γ generation, indicating that an infection is present since negative feedback
mechanisms were induced. PC2 did not inﬂuence the grouping.
Figure 36: PCA- Memory vs. Eﬀector Principal component analysis of all sample, except the two

Figure 37 shows a scatterplot with the average counts of the genes in the two groups
plotted against each other (Figure 37). The three highly expressed genes (Lcp2, B2m,
CD74) were excluded as they were mostly equally expressed and it gave a better visu-
alization. Genes with lower than four-fold gene expression diﬀerence were marked and
focus will thus be on the genes that diﬀer more than four-fold in expression levels. More
genes above background were observed within genes favored by the eﬀector group. Either
indicating that they were more stimulated or appeared as a result of the possibly higher
input. Another observation was that the eﬀector group exhibit high expression of genes,
which are not normally expected to be expressed by T cells, whereas the memory group
shows expression of more T cell-associated genes. This observation relate to the diﬀerent
methods used for isolation of speciﬁc CD4+ T cells.
==PAGE:76==
Results
57
The samples from the memory group were sorted had a very high purity of M.tb (ESAT-6
or TB10.4)-speciﬁc CD4+ T cells. The M.tb (ESAT-6 or TB10.4)-speciﬁc CD4+ T cells
in the eﬀector group was only isolated by two subsequent tetramer pull-downs (Flow
data was not applicable due to laboratory error). Tetramer pull-down does not isolate
cells to the same extent as sorting, and the samples within this group would, therefore,
most likely contain other cell types than M.tb speciﬁc CD4+ T cells, inclusive monocytes,
DCs, and macrophages. The genes not associated with T cell expression, observed in the
eﬀector group was possibly expressed by these other immune cells. They can be relevant
during a M.tb infection, by contributing to the innate immune response, T cell activation,
or cellular recruitment. The protein encoded by C1q has been seen to be highly expressed
in humans with active TB, and has been suggested to be a potential biomarker for active
TB [81]. The presence of this gene during a M.tb infection in the lungs supported this.

IFN-γ was not as highly expressed as could have been expected. However, there was an
expression of Cxc10, which is stimulated by an IFN-γ response. Indicating that IFNγ
presence.
A reason for the low amount of IFN-γ was probably the presence of Sox3
and Irf1 which encodes regulatory proteins with negative feedback on IFN-γ. Other T
cell-associated genes identiﬁed in the eﬀector group were associated with activation and
migration, which was consistent with an immune response against active disease (Fig-
ure 38). The memory group favored genes were more associated with T cell expression.
Including genes coding for proteins involved in TCR signaling and therefore T cell activa-
tion such as Lck, Cd3d, Ptprc. Most importantly the memory favored genes also included
genes associated with memory T cells; IL7r and Ccl5. There were also genes promoting
INF-γ production (Il18r1, Ifng) which does not match well with a memory respond. How-
ever, the memory counts in this plot were the average between H56/CAF01 and BCG
immunization samples, and the INF-γ expression might be related to BCG primed CD4+
T cells. The memory group also showed expression of genes not associated with T cell
expression. Genes such as S100a9 and S100a8, coding for the migratory inhibitory factor
related protein, mostly thought to be myeloid cell-derived proteins.
Despite low comparability between the eﬀector and memory group, comparing these two
populations did give an insight into the performance of the two isolation methods, showing
that tetramer pull-down was less eﬃcient, resulting in presence of other cell types than
CD4+ T cells, which makes the result less precise. It was also revealed that the input
amount, despite normalization, might inﬂuence the level of gene expression. Information
about the diﬀerences between eﬀector and memory response was also obtained, including
the expression of memory associated genes (Il7r, Cch5) in the memory group. The eﬀector
group showed expression of genes related to activation and migration, as well as the active
TB marker C1q and genes involved in negative feedback of INF-γ.
==PAGE:78==
Results
59
5.3.2
BCG vs. H56
The memory and the eﬀector group included samples from mice immunized with BCG
or H56/CAF01 in order to compare the eﬀects of the vaccines at a gene expression level.
However, due to the low level of information, that could be extracted from the data,
because of the low gene count, it was diﬃcult to identify any signiﬁcant diﬀerence. A gene
that is known to diﬀer to its expression level between T cells primed by the two vaccines
is the gene for the exhaustion marker KLRG1. BCG immunization has shown to prime
responses with increased expression of KLRG1 relative to H56/CAF01 [43]. However,
this gene was not a part of the CodeSet, and no information could be taken from the
gene expression analysis. Although, it was included in the surface stain for the memory
group and as inferred from the ﬂow data (Appendix D) BCG antigen-speciﬁc CD4+ T
cells expressed higher levels KLRG1 than the H56/CAF01 antigen-speciﬁc CD4+ T cells.
The higher amount of KLRG1 indicates that the BCG primed CD4+ T cells were more
diﬀerentiated along the TH1 axis than the H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells. The ﬂow
data also showed a higher amount of CX3CR1 in BCG samples suggesting they were more
associated with lung vasculature than lung parenchyma [12]. In addition to increased
KLRG1 expression is BCG primed CD4+ T cells also associated with increased T-bet
expression and a loss of IL-2 secretion. H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells is associated
with IL-2 and IL-17A secretion and expression of CXCR3 and PD1 [43]. No expression
was observed for these genes, which does not mean they were not there, only that they
were below background and therefore nondetectable. Nevertheless, the gene expression
analysis could still give some insight into the diﬀerence between the phenotypes of CD4+
T cells primed by the two vaccines. The comparisons between H56/CAF01 primed CD4+
T cells and BCG primed CD4+ T cells were made both in the memory group and in the
eﬀector group.
Memory
Besides the three genes in common between all samples (B2m, Cd74, and Lcp2) the
heatmap in Figure 39 shows that there were ﬁve genes in common across all memory
samples: Arhgdib, Cd4, Ets1, Nfkbia, and Tnfaip3. The high expression of Cd4 was ex-
pected as there were sorted for CD4+ T cells. The equal expression in both immunization
groups showed that puriﬁcation was done equally good. Arhgdib, Nfkbia, Tnfaip3, and
Ets1 might contribute to the memory state, as they can be involved in activation and/or
regulation of T cells and their functions.
Sample 3 had a low amount of expression,
correlating with low RNA concentration and 28S/18S ratio. Expression diﬀerences of
CD4+ memory T cells primed by BCG or H56/CAF01 was observed. The right side of
the heatmap shows that the BCG samples had more genes with expression above back-
ground than the H56/CAF01 samples. This could correlate to the fact that BCG is a
live bacterium which persists longer and could lead to prolonged antigen stimulation.

response against M.tb but are not customarily expressed by T cells. This indicated the
presence of other cell types or that T cells does have some expression of these genes.

BCG primed CD4+ T cells showed most genes with expression above background. The
genes favored to the BCG group were associated with high activation state and circu-

VLA-1 (α1β1) and VLA-4 (α4β1), associated with lymphocyte migration to the lungs,

Results
From this analysis, it was inferred that memory CD4+ T cells primed by BCG immuniza-
tion possess a higher activation status and express higher levels of migration receptors
than memory CD4+ T cells primed H56/CAF01 immunization. The expression proﬁle of
BCG CD4+ memory T cells, therefore, seems more in line with a response dominated by
eﬀector memory cells of a higher diﬀerentiation state, whereas the H56/CAF01 promotes
a more central memory response.
Eﬀector
The comparison within the eﬀector group was more challenging, due to the possible
concentration variations between sample input, and the lower number of replicates. The
heatmap (Figure 42), showed that sample 9 and 11 had higher expression, correlating with
the possible higher input for these samples. Sample 10 generally showed low expression,
although, the housekeeping counts did not show a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the
two H56/CAF01 samples (sample 9 and 10) for sample 10 to be taken out. The two
saline samples were treated with diﬀerent tetramers; sample 7 with MHC II ESAT-6
speciﬁc tetramers and sample 8 with TB10.4 speciﬁc tetramers, there was therefore not
a valid control sample for the BCG sample. The low number of replicates resulted in
minimal statistical evidence for the observations that could be made from this analysis,
the following results can therefore only be seen as indications of the actual situation.
