Green marking is genes with lower than a four-fold diﬀerence between the two
immunizations. The genes B2m, Cd74, H2-Ab1, H2-Aa, and Lcp2 was excluded for better visualization
(All have a high expression but correlated expression). R
==PAGE:83==
64
Results
The genes highly expressed in the BCG sample, but still expressed at detectable levels in
the H56/CAF01 group included several genes for migratory receptors such as Percam1,
CD97, Itgb1, Cd99. Several genes were highly expressed in the BCG sample, but below
background in the H56/CAF01 group. These included several genes coding for proteins
involved in T cell activation. One of these was Ifngr1, which in association with Ifngr2,
can form a IFN-γ receptor (Ifngr). However, neither Ifngr2 nor IFN-γ were expressed
above background. When taken into consideration that the BCG sample only consisted
of one sample, and previously suggestions for IFN-γ presence, the expression of Ifngr1
can be another indication that there was an IFN-γ response. Even with the lack of IFN-
γ and Ifng2, which could have been expressed at a lower level and not detected in this
particular setting. CD82, a costimulator for TCR signaling, was only expressed in the
BCG sample, suggesting that this sample consisted of highly activated CD4+ T cells,
compared to the H56/CAF01 group.
5.3.3
Gene Expression Analysis Conclusion
The result has shown that high cell-type purity is essential to secure precise information
from a gene expression analysis. Comparing the expressions from the H56/CAF01 group
with the expression from the BCG group showed that CD4+ T cell primed by BCG are
more activated and express more and higher levels of migratory receptors associated with
the prolonged antigen stimulation during BCG immunization. These T cells also showed
high diﬀerentiation status compared to H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cell which was more
associated with TCMs and was less diﬀerentiated. Few of the well-known markers were
found to be expressed, and related to the low gene count possibly due to insuﬃcient
ampliﬁcation. However, there were found diﬀerences of gene expression between CD4+ T
cells primed by H56/CAF01 vs. BCG, which might be interesting for further investigation
in T cell responses between the two immunizations.
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 6
Discussion
The goal of this thesis was to identify diﬀerences in gene expression of resting memory
or secondary eﬀector CD4+ T cells primed by BCG vs. H56/CAF01 immunization, with
the purpose of ﬁnding new markers associated with protection, to use in future TB vac-
cine research.
M.tb infects alveolar macrophages, and the adaptive immune response
mainly consists of TH1 CD4+ T cells, producing IFN-γ, stimulating the activation of
the infected macrophages [32]. The immune system can control the bacterium inside
a granuloma, leading to LTBI. However the bacteria is rarely entirely eradicated, and
reactivation can occur. Even if the infection is eradicated or after successful treatment,
protective immunity is not seen after a M.tb infection [43]. Developing a vaccine that
can induce a protective memory response is, therefore, a necessity for the eradication
of TB. Control of a M.tb infection has been linked to less diﬀerentiated CD4+ T cells
which can transmigrate from the vasculature and enter lung parenchyma [3]. However,
what constitutes a fully protective response has not yet been determined, but it might
depend on several factors. The BCG vaccine is a widely used, live attenuated vaccine,
shown to be able to initiate a protective TH1 response. With waning eﬃcacy over a
decade from administration in men. Likewise in mice, BCG can control the infection for
several weeks, but protection is lost with the development of late-stage chronic disease.
These attributes of the BCG vaccine is probably due to a prolonged antigen availability
and inﬂammatory signaling, leading to highly diﬀerentiated T cells, with poor ability to
home into the lung parenchyma [2, 43]. H56/CAF01 is a non-replicating subunit vaccine
and is thereby cleared faster, and has been shown to mediate a sustained protection [3].
Investigations of the gene expression diﬀerences between CD4+ T cells primed by either
BCG or H56/CAF01 can identify new markers associated with sustained protection.
Objective 1: Experimental Design
The ﬁrst objective of this thesis was to design an experimental procedure for the gene ex-
pression analysis. Several aspects must be considered, such as the type of gene expression
assay, puriﬁcation of RNA, and target cell isolation. Decisions and implementation must
also take other external factors such as cost, time, procedure eﬃciency and eﬀectiveness
into consideration.
==PAGE:85==
66
Discussion
The most widely used techniques for gene expression assays are quantitative PCR (qPCR),
microarrays and RNAseq, each with strengths and weaknesses. qPCR is cheap and the
most simple of the three, using PCR and ﬂuorophores for quantiﬁcation. It is limited
by speciﬁcity, as only known targets can be measured, due to the requirement of speciﬁc
primers. This method is best when analyzing expression of a few genes with a known
sequence in a candidate gene approach. Microarrays can examine expression levels of a
more substantial number of genes simultaneously. Targets are labeled with ﬂuorescent
dye and hybridized to the microarray for detection. It still depends on prior sequence
knowledge. However, it is a very aﬀordable and robust method. RNA-seq can look at a
broader range of expressions, and discover new genes. It does not depend on any prior
sequence knowledge, and all genes are sequenced; known or unknown [82]. It creates
a big dataset that can be complex to analyze, but signiﬁcant advantage is that it can
be reanalyzed upon new discoveries. The cost for RNA-seq is decreasing, yet still con-
siderably more expensive than the other methods and requires a signiﬁcant amount of
starting material. Nanostring technology that was used in this project is a variation of
microarrays. It can detect counts from a large target gene panel in one reaction using
color-coded target speciﬁc barcodes, which can be quantiﬁed by imaging. Compared to
RNAseq, Nanostring assays are cheap and simple to use. However, it depends on prior
sequence knowledge, and it is therefore not possible to discover new genes. Due to its
aﬀordability, and with concern to the limited amount of time for this thesis this simpler
analysis was chosen.
The input for the gene expression assay should consist of an isolated cell type, to en-
sure an accurate analysis. Isolation of a speciﬁc cell type can be done using FACS, which
by ﬂow cytometry can sort cells with speciﬁc ﬂuorescent labeling. As it detects every
single cell in a sample, an enrichment prior to cell sorting can ensure a more eﬃcient
cell sorting. However, it creates an extra step in the experimental procedure, with ap-
purtenant sources of error. In this thesis, cell sorting was done externally, as no FACS
was available at SSI, and it was billed hourly, making an eﬃcient sorting preferable. The
enrichment was performed by antigen speciﬁc MHC II tetramer staining and subsequent
magnetically pull-down. When using MHC II tetramers, it is essential to ensure optimal
staining conditions. Tetramer performance can decrease with time, as they can become
unstable, although the degree of this is batch dependent. A memory response consists of
a low pool of cells, and it should, therefore, be ensured that the tetramers perform at their
optimum, so a minimum of cells is lost during tetramer pull-down. During this thesis,
the procedure of tetramer staining and pull-down was optimized to fulﬁll the requirement
for tetramer conditions. The optimization also included the order of new tetramers after
the initial experiments using tetramers from 2016 showed mediocre results. After opti-
mizations, it was possible to perform suﬃcient tetramer pull-down. The new tetramers,
from 2017, gave good results in the following experiments.
==PAGE:86==
Discussion
67
Samples from M.tb infected animals require handling in a BSL-3 facility, in the course
of the thesis a FACS within a BSL-3 facility was not available, and samples within the
eﬀector group could not be enriched by sorting. To ensure purity of MHC II antigen-
speciﬁc CD4+ T cells were these samples were enriched by applying the ﬂowthrough of
the tetramer pull-down on new columns for a second pull-down. Although this highly
enhanced cell purity, this enrichment procedure is subservient to cell sorting. Which was
also clearly seen when comparing the gene expression of the two groups. The eﬀector
samples showed expression of genes that are not associated with T cell expression, im-
plying the presence of other cell types in the samples. Low purity makes it diﬃcult to
determine the cause of interesting gene expression diﬀerences, and ensuring high purity
will eliminate factors that could create changes in gene expression.
The Nanostring assay requires RNA in a suﬃcient quantity and quality. As described,
the number of vaccine-primed memory CD4+ T cells available are low. When purifying
RNA from a low number of cells, it is essential to ensure optimal performance, as no ma-
terial can be lost. RNases are released when the cells are lysed, and they will immediately
start to degrade RNA. In high material puriﬁcations, some RNA degradation is tolerable,
however, in low material puriﬁcations, it is essential to ensure eﬃcient and instantaneous
RNase degradation. In this experiment, it was ensured by addition of a reducing agent
to the lysis buﬀer. Another aspect that becomes important in low material RNA puriﬁ-
cation is the elution volume. When a certain concentration is needed for downstream
assays, it is important that the samples are not diluted unnecessarily. The membrane of
Nucleospin RNA XS columns has a smaller diameter compared to other kits, eg. RNeasy
Micro kit. The smaller diameter makes it possible to elute with a lower volume. By
using the Nucleospin RNA XS kit, the samples were eluded in 8µl. In the results showed
the importance of correct RNA concentration measurements as well as the diﬃculties in
measuring RNA at low concentrations. Knowing the correct concentration of an RNA
sample is essential for downstream procedures as it ensures equal sample inputs across
samples. Higher or lower concentrations than expected could create a false indication
gene expression. It is diﬃcult to measure the correct RNA concentration in a sample
with low amount of RNA. It is important to use an instrument which can determine
RNA concentration at the expected range. It could be advised to measure RNA concen-
trations with diﬀerent kits covering diﬀerent ranges as well as comparing concentrations
measured by diﬀerent instruments, eg. Bioanalyzer vs. Nanodrop.
Despite optimizations in cell isolation and RNA puriﬁcation, the RNA samples obtained
still had a too low concentration to be used in the Nanostring assay and ampliﬁcation
by MTE was needed before hybridization. When performing ampliﬁcation, it is essen-
tial to choose the right number of ampliﬁcation cycles, to ensure adequate amounts of
amplicons, yet within the linear range.
==PAGE:87==
68
Discussion
Too few ampliﬁcation cycles result in a low input for all genes, which cannot hence, be
detected in the assay. However, to many ampliﬁcation cycles can lead to misrepresen-
tation of clonal diﬀerences and artiﬁcial diversity, due to the ampliﬁcation bias. It is
recommended to perform optimizations on the ampliﬁcation procedure. Ampliﬁcation
with a diﬀerent number of cycles should have been tested. With these diﬀerent ampli-
ﬁcations runs, the optimal conditions can be found, and the right amount of cycles can
be chosen. To check the ampliﬁcation performance, test samples should have been taken
before and after ampliﬁcation and measured on a Qubit or with a Bioanalyzer DNA chip
to determine the amount of DNA. Due to time constrains as well as the volume of RNA
available was too low to perform several ampliﬁcation cycles, an ampliﬁcation optimiza-
tion could not be performed in this thesis. The eﬀector samples could have been used,
to test the RNA concentration estimate and after that applying the obtained knowledge
to the memory samples. A drawback in this strategy is that there might be a diﬀerence
in optimal conditions between the eﬀector and the memory samples. At the point of
ampliﬁcation, the time left for experimental procedures was minimal, which is another
reason for the lack of optimization at this step.
The data from the gene expression assay showed low gene counts across all samples,
which most likely was due to an insuﬃcient number of ampliﬁcation cycles. The low
data count makes it diﬃcult to interpret the result. There are some genes with expres-
sion above background, indicating that these genes are highly expressed, and genes with
lower expression simply could not overcome the background noise. Other factors also
inﬂuence the result and the interpretation. There are several diﬀerences between the 12
samples, which creates issues when comparing them. When analyzing gene expression
between two samples, the conditions for those sample should be similar, to avoid any
factors that can inﬂuence the result. Some samples are pooled together to obtain enough
cells for ensuring quality and quantity of RNA; pooling groups lose some of the biological
variations. The samples should either all be pooled or all be individual samples. There
are only three mice per immunization in the memory group and only two samples per
immunization in the eﬀector group. More replicates would give more precise results as
replicates inﬂuence the mean/variance relationship. In gene expression analysis, there
will be genes that are more highly expressed than others. These highly expressed genes
will vary in expression from sample to sample more than genes with lower expression.
More replicas would give a more precise estimate of group means.
However the cost
of having many replica needs to be taken into consideration. The input mass for the
Nanostring assay is minimum 1ng. However due to the low RNA concentrations, did the
memory samples only contain 0.7ng, whereas the eﬀector group contained 2ng. This can
also aﬀect the interpretation of the result.
==PAGE:88==
Discussion
69
The memory and eﬀector samples came from diﬀerent tissues. The eﬀector samples was
not initially a part of the experimental design, but as the possibility of acquiring in-
fected organs from immunized groups arose, it was found interesting to add. Only lungs
were available, at the time which is the reason for the diﬀerent tissues. However, not
comparable to the memory samples, this created an interesting insight into the eﬀect
of the immunizations in secondary eﬀectors. Analyzing cells from M.tb infected lungs
can gain an insight into the immune response at site of infection. The memory samples
derived from spleens, an essential part of the immune system, ﬁltering the blood and
lymphocyte storage, gave an insight into the composition of the speciﬁc CD4+memory
cells in SLOs. There would be a diﬀerence in what subset of memory T cells it is pos-
sible to detect. SLO contains residing TCMs and circulating TEMs, but TRMs from the
site of infection would not be detectable. The opposite would exist in the eﬀector samples.
The comparison of the eﬀector group and the memory group was used to determine
if the counts detected were a result of biology or just coincidence. It was found that the
gene expression that was seen must be a result of biology. The eﬀector samples showed
expression of genes related to activation and migration, conﬁrming their eﬀector status.
The memory samples expressed memory associated genes. One thing stands out, the
IFN-γ expression. High expression in the eﬀector group would have been expected, yet
it is expressed at a higher level in the memory group. However, there are genes in the
eﬀector group indicating that there is or have been an IFN-γ response, just not detected.
These genes include Cxcr10 which are stimulated by IFN-γ and genes that codes for
proteins involved in regulation of IFN-γ, indicating that there is a negative feedback,
possibly due to a high or prolonged IFN-γ response. IFN-γ is highly associated with a
TH1 response, however it has been suggested that high production of IFN-γ may not
correlate with protection[12]. When comparing the memory samples, it was shown that
the IFN-γ expression observed in the memory group was associated with BCG primed
CD4+ memory T cells, which might correlating with their higher activation status and
lower protection ability.
Objective 2: BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed resting memory CD4+ T cells
The second, but primary, objective was to investigate diﬀerences between phenotypes of
BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed resting memory CD4+ T cells. Immediate after immu-
nization, antigen will be present in high amounts. In time, the antigen will no longer be
present, and the number of immune cells will decrease signiﬁcantly. It is here the memory
cells are developed. Leaving a population of antigen-speciﬁc memory T cells. As BCG
is a live bacterium it will persist longer, leading to a prolonged antigen stimulation, and
thereby more T cell activation. The memory response was in this experiment tested at
week 11-14 post-immunization, consistent with a memory response. The mice from week
14 might have a smaller pool of memory cells, but it should not be too signiﬁcant.
==PAGE:89==
70
Discussion
The comparison between gene expression of the BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed CD4+
memory T cells, overall, showed that the BCG primed CD4+ memory T cells had a
higher expression in a larger amount of genes compared to H56/CAF01 primed CD4+
memory T cells. This correlates with the expected more activated status BCG primed
CD4+ memory T cells, and it indicates that H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ memory T cells
had a more resting phenotype.
Most of the known markers for deﬁning the memory
response induced by these vaccines were not detected in the gene expression analysis,
due to the low expression level. It would have provided a good basis for comparing re-
sults with other studies and ensuring the presence of vaccine-induced CD4+ memory T
cells. However ﬂow data did conﬁrm increased KLRG1 and CX3CR1 expression of BCG
primed CD4+ memory T cells, which is consistent with other studies [43].
Deﬁning the memory T cell subtype is also associated with well-known markers, and
is a combination of speciﬁc markers for each subtype, which should be expressed in a sit-
uation without infection [44]. Again due to the low expression level not all of these genes
were detected, which made it diﬃcult to determine the subtype of memory cells the two
immunizations induced. However, some diﬀerences in the memory response between the
CD4+ memory T cells primed by the two vaccines could be observed. H56/CAF01 primed
CD4+ T cells expressed high levels of Il7r (CD127), associated with long-lived memory
cells and might be TCMs, resting in the spleen. This is in correlation with published
data showing H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells are less diﬀerentiated and associated
with TCMs [3]. The genes highly expressed by BCG primed CD4+ T cells were genes
associated with activation, eﬀector functions, and especially migratory behavior. This
again conﬁrms as previously suggested for the BCG vaccine, that these cells are possibly
more activated, circulating and belong to the TEMs subset. The circulation of these
cells could explain why the BCG vaccine initially provide good protection as performing
good immunosurveillance, whereas the highly activated and diﬀerentiated state can be
explanatory of why the protection is short lived and not sustained. The circulation could
also contribute to the activation/diﬀerentiated state, as it leads to higher antigen avail-
ability. Migration into the lung parenchyma correlated with protection and associated
with H56/CAF01 induced memory response. These aspects make migration a interesting
part of the memory response. The observed CD4+ memory T cells seen in the result is
resting, and it cannot be predicted what exact kind of migration they promote, as some
migration receptors ﬁrst will be expressed upon activation [34].
Objective 3: BCG vs. H56/CAF01 secondary eﬀector CD4+ T cells
The third objective of this thesis was to investigate diﬀerences between phenotypes of
BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed secondary eﬀector CD4+ T cells. Due to sample variations,
the lower replication and an insuﬃcient cell type isolation, it was diﬃcult to compare the
two immunizations in the eﬀector group.
==PAGE:90==
Discussion
71
The H56 group showed very low expression of T cell associated genes, this could either
be due to insuﬃcient isolation of vaccine-primed CD4+ T cells or to biological causes.
Which stressed the importance of proper cell isolation prior to gene expression analy-
sis, in order not to be confounded by diﬀerential cell inﬂux. A few diﬀerences in the
secondary eﬀector response between the CD4+ eﬀector T cells primed by the two vac-
cines could be observed. A possible TH17 response was observed in the H56/CAF01
group which can be associated with CAF01, indicating that the vaccine had an impact
on the immune response. The BCG primed CD4+ T cells showed expression of some of
the same migration markers expressed by BCG primed CD4+ resting memory T cells,
such as Itgb1 and Itgal. Suggesting they are involved in the immune response against
the real M.tb infection. It would have been interesting to compare this ﬁnding with the
H56/CAF01 group, however it was not possible due to the insecurities of these samples.
Another interesting comparison would have been with an non-immunized control group.
Unfortunately, this was sample 8, which was lost due to a laboratory error. An accurate
comparison between BCG vs. H56/CAF01 primed secondary eﬀector CD4+ T cells was
not possible, but it is an interesting comparison to make, as it can determine the full
functions of a vaccine-induced memory response.
Objective 4: New Markers
The last objective was to identify any possible new markers for vaccine-primed CD4+ T
cell associated protection against M.tb. The low expression and variations in sample input
made it diﬃcult to identify new interesting genes. Nevertheless, there were genes that dif-
fered between the two immunizations that do not typically come up in memory response
experiments. This includes several migration genes, one of these is Itgb1, expressed in
high levels in both immunization groups. It codes for integrin-β1, which is a part of VLA-
1 and VLA-4 associated with migration to the lungs. Repeating this experiment to see
if the result could be reproduced, as well as comparing it with a non-immunized control
group, could determine if it is a conserved expression and if it has importance for the
memory response against M.tb. Two other migration associated genes, Itgb2 and Itgal
forming LFA-1, was only expressed in the BCG primed CD4+ memory T cells. LFA-1 is
important for migration into tissues and the SLOs, and is therefore a possible marker for
BCG circulation. It could be interesting to investigate if the expression of these genes
could provide better protection. However such investigations should also determine if a
higher circulation provide a higher diﬀerentiation of the T cells, which is not favorable
for a long-term memory response. As already mentioned were Il7r (CD127) expressed at
higher levels in memory cells than in eﬀector cells, it further showed that it had a higher
expression in H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells than BCG primed. CD127 is already
known to be important for a memory response, but have mostly been studied in CD8+ T
cells. It could be interesting to look further into its inﬂuence on memory CD4+ T cells,
and why it is mostly expressed in H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells.
==PAGE:91==
72
Discussion
Perspectives
The results shows that a gene expression assay is an interesting approach, to gain a more
detailed view of the response primed by immunizations. Future gene expression experi-
ments concerning M.tb and vaccine-primed CD4+ memory T cells could provide valuable
knowledge, for understanding the factors involved in a protective response against M.tb.
Such knowledge could include the role of migration and location of CD4+ T cells, includ-
ing a fully determination of the mechanisms that enable T cells to home to the lung and
enter the lung parenchyma. Expression patterns of memory T cell could result in an in-
creased understanding of the memory subset functioning and the mechanism behind their
development. To be able to identify and understand factors relating to protection against
M.tb infection can be an essential asset for future TB vaccine research. Precondition for
success of such an experiments are to ensure proper conditions for the gene expression
analysis as discussed above. In designing a new experiment, a customized CodeSet could
be an option, containing well-known genes associated with CD4+ T cell response towards
M.tb . In doing so, the results could be compared to other studies, regarding the CD4+
T cell response. Nevertheless, a customized CodeSet could create biases in the experi-
ment, though still be a potent tool in conﬁrming known ﬁndings and possible relations.
If the goal of the experiment is to discover new markers, a broader investigation should
be made. RNAseq can give information of all genes known or unknown, without biases.
Using single-cell expression proﬁling, instead of bulk samples, can make the result more
speciﬁc, as the exact type of cell proﬁled is known. An approach could be to uses cell
lysates directly, and thereby avoid RNA extraction and create a more straightforward
procedure with fewer sources of errors.
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
Sustained control of a M.tb infection has been linked to less diﬀerentiated CD4+ T cells
with the ability to transmigrate from the vasculature and enter infected tissue, and form
populations of TCMs. However, what fully constitutes a protective immune response has
not yet been determined. In this study gene expression analysis was used to identify
and understand factors involved in CD4+ T cell memory functions, that correlates to
protection against M.tb infection. The input for the gene expression analysis was RNA
puriﬁed from MHC II Tetramer sorted CD4+ memory T cells, and MHC II Tetramer
enriched CD4+ eﬀector T cells, primed by BCG or H56/CAF01 immunization. After
immunization, a low amount of memory cells will be available, leading to small amounts of
RNA. Obtaining RNA from memory cells in suﬃcient quantity and quality was, therefore,
setting the bar high.
All procedures needed to be performed optimally.
This meant
several optimizations was needed. Especially the performance of tetramer staining, RNA
degradation, and suﬃcient ampliﬁcation was shown to be essential factors. Due to time
limitations for the study, optimization was not carried out for the ampliﬁcation procedure.
The result showed low gene count primary reason being limited number of ampliﬁcation
cycles. The data may, therefore, only have consisted of highly expressed genes, whereas
lower expressed genes were not detected.
The primary focus was to investigate expression diﬀerences of H56/CAF01 vs.
BCG
primed CD4+ T cells. The BCG primed CD4+ T cells showed a higher activation and
thereby higher diﬀerentiation state, with higher expression levels of migratory markers
than the H56/CAF01 primed CD4+ T cells. Some genes which are not typically em-
ployed in memory response experiments were found to be candidates for further research.
These genes included migration associated genes such as Itgb1 (VLA-1 and VLA-4), and
Itgb2/Itgal (LFA-1). The memory marker Il7r (CD127), was expressed by H56/CAF01
primed CD4+ memory T cells, and is an important marker as the homeostatic pro-
liferation of TCMs depends on it. Focus on these genes might be considered for future
experiments. It is indicated that gene expression analysis can provide expression patterns
of speciﬁc cells, that can be used for understanding the factors involved in a protective
response against M.tb. Careful planning and execution of experiments are crucial for
optimal results.

Appendix B
Procedures
B.1
Vaccine Preparation
H56/CAF01:
1. Mix antigen (H56), Tris buﬀer and CAF01 (Figure 44) in sterile tube.
2. Shake.
3. Rest for 1h.
4. Deliver to Animal Facility in sterile bag.
Control:
1. Add Tris buﬀer to sterile tube (Figure 44).
2. Deliver tube to animal facility in sterile bag.
Figure 44: Vaccine Calculations. Amount of additives for H56 and control immunizations.
BCG:
1. Thaw BCG in water bath.
2. Place in an ultrasound bath for 5 min. for sonication.
3. Use sterile suction needle for homogenization.
4. Dilute BCG in PBS (Figure 45) in sterile tube.
5. Make 3 dilution and plate them out on 7H11 plates X2.
6. Deliver tube to animal facility in sterile bag.
7. Let the plate with dilutions stand for 1h.
8. Incubate plate with dilutions at 37◦C for 14 days.
9. Count CFUs and calculate actual concentration (Figure 46).

B.2
Tetramer Pull-down - Memory
Organ preparation:
1. Empty tubes through cell strainer placed in well on a 6 well plate.
2. Use syringes to force spleen through cell strainer.
3. Fill tube with PBS (total of ∼12ml).
4. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
5. Resuspend with 12ml RMPI Wash.
6. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
7. Resuspend with 800 RPMI with Hepes + Cell supplement + 10% FCS.
8. Force through cell strainer twice.
9. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant
Tetramer Staining and Pull-down:
10. Resuspend in 200µl Tetramer staining + Fc block.
11. Strain at 37◦C for 30 min.
12. Add 4ml ice coldFACS buﬀer .
13. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
14. Resuspend cells in 250µl ice cold magnetic beads .
15. Strain at 4◦C for 30 min.
16. Wash by adding 5ml ice cold FACS buﬀer.
17. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant
18. Resuspend in ∼350µl ice cold FACS buﬀer and apply/run on LS columns
19. Re-apply/run ﬂowthrough.
20. Wash column 3X with 3ml ice cold FACS buﬀer.
21. Remove columns from magnet and place them in collection tubes.
22. Press 5ml ice cold FACS buﬀer through columns, using the plunger.
23. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
Surface Staining:
21. Resuspend samples in 100µl Surface Stain.
22. Strain at 4◦C for 30 min.
23. Wash by adding 2.5ml FACS buﬀer.
24. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
25. Wash by adding 2.5ml FACS buﬀer.
26. Spin down at 1800 rpm for 5 min. →Discard supernatant.
27. Resuspend in 500µl.
28. Put on ice
29. Sort cells on a Aria II FACS

B.3
RNA puriﬁcation - NucleoSpin RNA XS
Lyse and Homogenize cells:
1. Make Master-mix: 100µl Buﬀer Ra1 and 2µl TCEP per sample.
2. Add 102 µl Master-mix
→Vortex vigorously (2x5 seconds).
Bind RNA and Desalt silica membrane:
3. Add 100µl 70% Ethanol
→Mix with pipette 5 times.
4. Transfer 202 µl lysate to Nucleospin RNA XS Columns.
5. Spin down at 10000 rpm for 1 min. →Transfer to new 2 ml RNase free tubes.
6. Add 100 µl MDB (desalts).
7. Spin down at 10000 rpm for 1 min. →Discard ﬂow-through reuse tubes.
DNase Treatment:
8. Prepare rDNase reaction mixture: 3µl rDNase to 27 µl Reaction buﬀer per sample.
9. Apply 25µl rDNase reaction mixture directly onto the center of the membrane.
10. Incubate 15 min. at room temp in bench.
Wash X 3:
11. Add 100µl RA2 Buﬀer.
12. Incubate 2 min at room temp in bench.
13. Spin down at 11000 rpm for 1 min. →Transfer to new 2 ml RNase free tubes.
14. Add 400µl RA3 Buﬀer.
15. Spin down at 11000 rpm for 1 min. →Discard ﬂow-through and reuse tube.
16. Add 200µl RA3 Buﬀer.
17. Spin down at 11000 rpm for 2 min. →Transfer to new 1.5 ml RNase free tubes.
Elute:
18. Add 10µl RNase-free water
19. Spin down at 11000 rpm for 2 min. →Remove columns and close lids.
20. Analyze on Bioanalyzer

B.6
Bioanalyzer (Agilent RNA 6000 Pico Kit )
Prepare Gel-Dye Mix
1. Set RNA dye concentrate at room temp. for 30 min.
2. Votex RNA dye concentrate for 10s and spin down 2 sek.
3. Add 1µl RNA dye concentrate to 1µl Filtered Gel.
4. Votex.
5. Spin down at 13000 rpm for 10 min.
Loading Chip
6. Put a new RNA chip on the chip priming station.
7. Pipette 9µl Gel-Dye Mix in the well marked G
see Figure 47.
8. Make sure that the plunger is positioned at 1 mL.
9. Close the chip priming station.
10. Press plunger until it is held by the clip.
11. Wait 30s and release clip.
12. Wait for 5s, Slowly pull back plunger to 1 mL position.
13. Pipette 9µl Gel-Dye Mix in the wells marked G.
14. Pipette 9µl RBA condition solution into the well marked CS.
15. Pipette 5µl RNA Marker in all 11 sample wells and in the ladder well.
16. Pipette 5µl Ladder in the ladder well.
17. Pipette 5µl sample in sample wells.
18. Put the chip horizontally in the IKA vortexer and vortex for 1 min at 2400 rpm.
19. Run in Bioanalyzer

Appendix C
Flourescence Spectra
When ﬂuorochrome-labeled antibodies on cells pass through a laser beam in ﬂow cytometry,
the ﬂuorochrome will absorb energy in the form of photons and go into to an excited electronic
state. The excited state is unstable, and they want to return to the more stable ground state, to
do this they must release the extra energy, most of this will be emitted as protons (light). The
emitted light is known as the ﬂuorochromes ﬂuorescence. During this transition, there is a loss of
energy, as the molecules in the excited state will vibrate and release energy in the form of heat.
The emission, therefore, has lower energy levels than the excitation, and the wavelength of the
emitted light will always be higher than the wavelength of the light that caused the excitation,
this diﬀerence is called Strokes Shift (Figure 48) [73]. Each ﬂuorochrome has an excitation and
an emission maximum, the excitation maximum is the wavelength of the light they are most
eﬃciently excited by and the emission maximum is the wavelength where the emission is most
likely to occur. Light with the wavelength close to the maxima can course excitation or emission
but less eﬀective or intense respectively [73].
Figure 48: Strokes shift. The diﬀerence in wavelength between the excitation and emission maxima.
A diﬀerence that arises as energy is lost at the unstable excited state due to molecular vibration. 1. The
excitation maximum/energy level of exited ﬂuorochrome, 2. Strokes Shift/The loss of energy as heat, 3.
Emission maximum/Energy level of emission (ﬂuorescence) [73].

orochromes used in this project. Figures 50,51, and 52 shows the emmision spectra for each
laser.
